Pre-emptor Taking from Seller vs Buyer on Deferred Price
Ruling on three scenarios of pre-emptor's payment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rulings when a pre-emptor takes property from the seller or buyer and identify the pre-emptor's right to wait.

Core Idea
The pre-emptor may take the property by paying cash immediately or wait until the original term ends.

In this lesson, we examine a key scenario from the chapter of shuf‘ah (pre-emption). When a pre-emptor exercises his right, he may acquire the property from the seller or the buyer. The price he pays—cash or deferred—depends on whom he takes it from. The Hanafi school also grants him the option to wait for the original payment term, protecting him from immediate financial burden. Let's walk through the three rulings detailed by the author.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties
The pre-emptor (shafi‘), the seller (ba’i‘), and the buyer (mushtari). The property was sold on a deferred price.
2
Scenario A: Pre-emptor takes from seller with cash
What happens to the buyer's debt? It is dropped entirely because the sale is rescinded for the buyer.
The pre-emptor stands in the buyer's place.
3
Scenario B: Pre-emptor takes from the buyer
The buyer still owes the seller the original deferred price. The condition of deferment is not invalidated by pre-emption.
The author compares this to someone buying on credit then selling for cash; deferment continues.
4
Understand the analogy
The author gives an example: buying on credit then selling for cash does not cancel the deferment. Similarly here.
5
The pre-emptor's option to wait
He may choose to wait until the term ends before paying. This is to avoid the harm of immediate cash payment.
Immediate taking is not obligatory; he has the right to wait.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم ان اخذها بثمن حال من البائع سقط الثمن عن المشترى لما بينا من قبل وان اخذها المشترى رجع البائع على المشترى بثمن مؤجل كما كان لان الشرط الذى جرى بينهما لم يبطل باخذ الشفيع فبقى موجبه فصار كما اذا بـاعـه بثمن حال وقد اشتراه مؤجلا وان اختار الانتطار له ذالك لان له ان لا يلتزم زيادة الضرر من حيث النقدية

ترجمہ پھر اگر شفیع نے اس مکان کو بائع سے نقد میں لے لیا تو مشتری کے اوپر سے ثمن ساقط ہو جائے گا۔ اس دلیل کی وجہ سے جس کو ہم ماقبل میں بیان کر چکے ہیں اور اگر اس کو مشتری نے لیا تو بائع مشتری پر جیسے تھا ویسے ہی ثمن موجل کے ساتھ رجوع کرے گا۔ اسلئے کہ وہ شرط جوان دونوں کے درمیان جاری ہوئی ہے وہ شفیع کے لینے کی وجہ سے باطل نہیں ہوئی تو اس کا حکم باقی ہے تو ایسا ہو گیا جیسے بیع فروخت کر دیا مبیع کو نقد ثمن کے ساتھ ۔ حالانکہ اس کو ادھار خریدا تھا اور اگر شفیع نے انتظار کو اختیار کیا تو اس کو اس کا حق ہے کہ نقد دینے کی حیثیت سے ضرر کی زیادتی کا التزام نہ کرے۔

تشریح .. جب شفیع اس مکان کو نقد قیمت دے کر لے گا تو دو صورتیں ہوں گی یا تو بائع سے لے گا یا مشتری سے۔ اگر بائع سے لیا ہو تو مشتری کے ذمہ جو بائع کا ثمن تھاوہ مشتری کے ذمہ سے ساقط ہو گیا۔ دلیل سابق کی وجہ سے کہ بیع مشتری کے حق میں فتح ہوگئی اور اب شفیع مشتری کا قائم مقام ہو گیا ہے اور عقد کے فسخ ہونے سے ثمن ساقط ہو جاتا ہے۔ اور اگر مشتری سے لیا ہو تو مشتری کے ذمہ جو ثمن تھا وہ بدستور ادھار رہے گا۔ اس لئے کہ مکان کے شفعہ میں چلے جانے کی وجہ سے شرط سابق باطل نہ ہوگی ۔ لہذا اس کا حکم علی حالہ باقی رہے گا اور اس کی مثال ایسی سمجھو کہ کسی نے کوئی چیز ادھار خریدی اور اس کو نقد فروخت کردی تو نقد فروخت کرنے کی وجہ سے اجل ختم نہیں ہوئی بلکہ وہ بدستور برقرار ہے ایسے ہی یہاں ہے۔

مصنف نے پھر ایک مسئلہ بتایا کہ شفیع چاہتا ہے کہ جب مدت ختم ہوگی اس وقت ثمن دے کر مکان لے لوں گا تو شفیع ایسا کرسکتا شفیع کو مجبور نہیں کیا جا سکتا کہ ابھی ثمن دے کر لو۔ اس کو کیونکہ شفیع پر اگر ابھی لینا لازم کر دیا جائے تو اس کو ضر ر لاحق ہو گا، کیسا ضرر؟ ہے۔ کیونکہ مکان تو اس کو اس قیمت پر مل ہی جائے گا جب مدت گذر جائے گی تو اس کو ابھی نقد دینے کے ضرر سے حفاظت ہو جائے گی۔ جب تک یہ اپنی رقم دوسری جگہ استعمال کر کے فائدہ حاصل کر سکتا ہے۔ اسلئے فی الفور لینا اس پر لازم نہ ہوگا بلکہ اس کو اختیار ہو گا کہ چاہے ابھی لے لے۔ مگر ثمن نقد ہوگا یا اجل کے گذرنے کا انتظار کرے۔
Then if he takes it for a cash price from the seller, the price is dropped from the buyer, as we stated before. And if the buyer takes it, the seller reverts upon the buyer for a deferred price as it was, because the condition that occurred between them is not invalidated by the pre-emptor's taking; its effect remains. So it becomes as if he sold it for a cash price while he had bought it on credit. And if he chooses to wait, he has that right, because he may not undertake the extra harm of paying cash.

Translation: Then if the pre-emptor takes the house for cash from the seller, the price owed by the buyer is dropped. For the reason we stated before. And if he takes it from the buyer, the seller will revert to the buyer for the same deferred price. Because the condition that was between them is not invalidated by the pre-emptor's taking, so its ruling remains. And it becomes like one who sold for cash what he had bought on credit. And if the pre-emptor chooses to wait, he has the right not to undertake the extra harm of paying cash.

Explanation: When the pre-emptor takes the house for cash, there are two scenarios: either he takes from the seller or from the buyer. If he takes from the seller, the debt from the buyer is dropped, because the sale is rescinded for the buyer, and the pre-emptor stands in his place, and the price is dropped upon rescission. If he takes from the buyer, the buyer's debt remains deferred, because the prior condition is not invalidated by the property going into pre-emption. Its ruling remains. And its example is like one who bought something on credit and then sold it for cash: the deferment is not terminated by the cash sale; it remains. Similarly here. The author then states another issue: if the pre-emptor wishes to take the property at the end of the term by paying the price then, he can do so. He cannot be forced to take it immediately, because that would cause him harm. What harm? Because he can secure the property at that price later, and he can use his money elsewhere until the term passes. Therefore, immediate taking is not obligatory upon him; he has the option to take now (with cash) or wait for the term to pass.
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement from the chapter of pre-emption (shuf‘ah). It explains the effect on the price when the pre-emptor takes the property, and whether he must pay immediately or can wait. The author first presents three scenarios: (1) If the pre-emptor takes from the seller directly, paying cash, the buyer's debt to the seller is dropped—because the sale is cancelled from the buyer's side. (2) If the pre-emptor takes from the buyer (i.e., the buyer was the original purchaser), the buyer's obligation to pay the seller remains as originally deferred—the condition of deferment is not invalidated by the pre-emptor's taking. The example is given: someone buys on credit then sells for cash; the deferment continues. (3) The pre-emptor may choose to wait until the original term expires before paying and taking possession. This is his right to avoid the harm of paying cash immediately. The ruling underscores that the pre-emptor is not forced to pay cash; he can wait, but if he takes immediately, the price is cash.

Key Terms
شفعة (shuf‘ah): Right of pre-emption in property sale
شفيع (shafi‘): Pre-emptor; the one who has the right of shuf‘ah
ثمن حال (thaman haal): Cash price payable immediately
ثمن مؤجل (thaman mu’ajjal): Deferred price payable at a future term

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If the pre-emptor takes the property from the seller (the original seller) by paying cash, what happens to the buyer's debt to the seller?
A) The buyer still owes the seller the deferred price.
B) The buyer's debt is dropped entirely.
C) The buyer must now pay cash to the pre-emptor.
D) The buyer pays the seller half the price.

Key Takeaway
The pre-emptor may take the property by paying cash or wait for the original term; the buyer's debt is cancelled if taken from seller but remains deferred if taken from buyer.

Ruling on Pre-emptor's Right: Buyer's Dispositions Are Void
Explains principle that pre-emptor's right overrides buyer's dispositions with qiyas response

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the buyer's unauthorized construction in pre-emption and explain the intra-madhhab dispute with Imam Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
Buyer's unauthorized construction in pre-empted property is demolished at buyer's cost.

This lesson examines a critical intra-Hanafi dispute: when a buyer builds on property subject to pre-emption, must the pre-emptor compensate him? The Zahir al-Riwayah position, supported by Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, holds that the building is demolished without compensation because the pre-emptor's right is stronger. Imam Abu Yusuf disagreed through qiyas, analogizing to gift and invalid sale. We will analyze the proofs, the rebuttal, and the practical consequence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
The issue is whether the pre-emptor (shafi') can have the buyer's (mushtari) construction demolished or must pay for it. The outcome hinges on the strength of the pre-emptor's right and whether the buyer was authorized.
2
State the Zahir al-Riwayah position
The majority (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) hold that any disposition by the buyer, including construction, sale, or gift, is void because the buyer was not authorized by the pre-emptor. The construction is demolished without compensation.
This is the position of the author (Ashraf al-Hidaya) and is mufta bihi.
3
State Imam Abu Yusuf's counter-argument
Abu Yusuf analogized pre-emption to gift and invalid sale (shira' fāsid). In those cases, the recipient (donee or buyer) is authorized by the right-holder, and if they build, the rightful claimant must compensate them. Abu Yusuf argued the same should apply to pre-emption.
His qiyas is based on a perceived similarity in the aftermath of building.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
The author replies that the analogy is invalid (ma‘a al-fāriq). In gift and invalid sale, the recipient was authorized by the right-holder (tasleet), whereas in pre-emption the buyer was not. Moreover, the right of return in gift and invalid sale is weak and does not survive building; the pre-emptor's right remains. Therefore, imposing a price on the pre-emptor is meaningless.
The response also cites the case of rightful owner reclaiming land with building (istihqāq) where no compensation is paid.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf and mufta bihi
Practical difference: Under Abu Yusuf, the buyer may keep the building and the pre-emptor pays; under the Zahir al-Riwayah, the building is removed at the buyer's cost. The relied-upon fatwa is the Zahir al-Riwayah: demolition without compensation.
This settles the dispute for practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحق کی جانب سے تسلیط کے بغیر تو اس کو توڑ دیا جائے گا۔ جیسے راہن جبکہ وہ مرہون میں مکان بنائے اور یہ اسلئے کہ اس کا حق مشتری کے حق سے زیادہ قوی ہے اسلئے کہ شفیع مشتری پر مقدم ہے۔ اور اسی وجہ سے اس کی بیع اور ہبہ اور اس کے علاوہ دیگر تصرفات تو ڑ دیئے جاتے ہیں۔
تشریح مصنف امام ابو یوسف کے دلائل سے فراغت کے بعد یہاں سے ظاہر الروایہ کی دلیل بیان کرتے ہیں کہ: اصول یہ ہے کہ اگر کسی چیز میں کسی کا حق ہے اور صاحب حق نے کسی کو مسلط نہیں کیا کہ اس میں کچھ تصرف کرے اور کوئی تصرف کر بیٹھے تو اس کے تصرف کو توڑ دیا جاتا ہے۔ جب یہ اصول مقرر ہے تو اب دیکھئے کہ یہ شفیع کا حق ہے اور شفیع نے مشتری کو حکم نہیں دیا کہ یہاں مکان تعمیر کرے اور اس نے کر دیا تو اس ضابطہ کے مطابق اس کے توڑے جانے کا حکم دیا جائے گا۔
اس کی مثال بعینہ ایسی ہے کہ راہن نے مرہون میں مکان بنا لیا۔ اگر چہ مرہون راہن کی ملکیت ہے اور اس میں مرتہن کا حق وابستہ ہے تو اس حق کی وابستگی کی وجہ سے راہن کے تصرف کو توڑ دیا جاتا ہے۔ ایسے ہی یہاں بھی تو ڑ دیا جائے گا، کیوں تو ڑا جائے گا ؟ اسلئے کہ شفیع مقدم ہے اور اس کا حق مشتری کے حق سے مضبوط ہے جب بات یوں ہے تو اگر مشتری نے اس کو فروخت کر دیا یا ہبہ کر دی یا کچھ اور تصرف کردیا ، اسکوتو ڑ دیا جائے گا۔
امام ابو یوسف کے قیاس کا جواب
بخلاف الهبة والشراء الفاسد عند ابي حنيفة لأنه حصل بتسليط من جهة من له الحق ولان حق الاسترداد فيهما ضعيف ولهذا لا يبقى بعد البناء و هذا الحق يبقى فلا معنى لا يجاب القيمة كمافي الاستحقاق
ترجمہ بخلاف جہیہ کے اور شراء فاسد سے ابو حنیفہ کے نزدیک اسلئے کہ یہ حاصل ہوا ہے من لہ الحق کی جانب سے تسلیط کی وجہ سے اور اسلئے کہ ان دونوں میں ( ہبہ اور شراء فاسد ) واپسی کا حق ضعیف ہے اور اسی وجہ سے یہ حق تعمیر کے بعد باقی نہیں رہے گا اور یہ حق ( شفعہ ) باقی رہے گا۔ تو ایجاب قیمت کے کوئی معنی نہیں جیسے استحقاق کی صورت میں ۔
تشریح امام ابو یوسف نے شفعہ کے مسئلہ کو ہبہ پر قیاس کیا تھا تو اس کا جواب دیا کہ یہ قیاس مع الفارق ہے کیونکہ ہبہ کے اندر تو موہوب لہ واہب کی جانب سے تصرف کرنے پر مسلط تھا اور مشتری شفیع کی جانب سے مسلط نہیں تھا۔ لہذا پھر قیاس کیوں کر صحیح ہوگا۔ اور اسی طرح شراء فاسد میں مشتری بائع کی جانب سے تصرفات پر مسلط تھا بخلاف مشتری اور شفیع کے۔ عند ابی حنیفہ کی قید کیوں ہے؟ اسلئے کہ واپسی کا حق نہ ہونا شراء فاسد میں امام ابو حنیفہ کا قول ہے۔ ورنہ صاحبین فرماتے ہیں کہ شراء فاسد میں جبکہ مشتری نے مشترکی پر مکان تعمیر کر لیا تو بائع کو واپس لینے کا حق ہے۔
بہر حال شفعہ کو ان دونوں پر قیاس کرنا صحیح نہیں ہے مذکورہ بالا دلیل کی وجہ سے اور اس دلیل کی وجہ سے کہ ہبہ اور شراء فاسد میں واپس لینا کمزور ہے اور شفعہ کے اندر بہر صورت مشتری سے واپس لینا درست ہے۔ بہر حال ہبہ اور شراء فاسد میں بعد بناء حق واپسی باقی نہیں رہتا اور شفعہ میں رہتا ہے تو جب استر داد شفیع کا حق ہے تو اس پر قیمت واجب کرنے کے کیا معنی ۔
اور اس کو ایسا سمجھو جیسے کسی نے کوئی جگہ خریدی اور اس پر مکان تعمیر کر دیا اور کسی نے دعوی کر دیا کہ اس زمین کا مالک میں ہوں ۔ قاضی نے مستحق کیلئے فیصلہ کر دیا تو مستحق اس زمین کو لے گا اور مشتری کی عمارت اکھڑوا دے گا اور مستحق پر کوئی ضمان یا کوئی قیمت واجب نہیں ہو گی ۔ البتہ مشتری کو حق ہے کہ وہ بائع کی جانب رجوع کرے اور اپنا شمن قیمت بناء بائع سے واپس لے۔
Without authorization from the right-holder, it will be demolished. Like when a mortgagor builds a house in the mortgaged property, and this is because his right is stronger than the buyer's right, because the pre-emptor takes precedence over the buyer. For this reason, his sale, gift, and other dispositions are invalidated.
Explanation: After finishing the proofs of Imam Abu Yusuf, the author here presents the proof of Zahir al-Riwayah: The principle is that if someone has a right in something and the right-holder has not authorized anyone to dispose of it, and someone does dispose, then that disposition is invalid. When this principle is established, now look: The pre-emptor has a right, and he did not authorize the buyer to build a house, but the buyer did so, so according to this rule, it will be ordered to be demolished.
This is exactly like the case of a mortgagor building a house in the mortgaged property. Although the mortgaged property is owned by the mortgagor, but the mortgagee has a right attached to it, so due to that attachment, the mortgagor's disposition is invalidated. Similarly here, it will be invalidated. Why will it be invalidated? Because the pre-emptor takes precedence and his right is stronger than the buyer's right. When this is the case, then if the buyer sold it, or gave it as a gift, or made any other disposition, it will be invalidated.
Reply to the Qiyas of Imam Abu Yusuf:
Contrary to gift and invalid sale, according to Abu Hanifa, because they are done with authorization from the right-holder, and because the right of recovery in them is weak, for this reason it does not remain after building, and this right (of pre-emption) remains, so there is no meaning to imposing the price, as in the case of rightful claim.
Translation: Contrary to gift and invalid sale, according to Abu Hanifa, because they are done with authorization from the right-holder, and because in both (gift and invalid sale) the right of return is weak, for this reason this right does not remain after building, but this right (of pre-emption) remains. So there is no meaning to imposing the price, as in the case of rightful claim.
Explanation: Imam Abu Yusuf had analogized the case of pre-emption to gift, so the reply is that this analogy is different because in gift, the donee was authorized by the donor to dispose, but the buyer was not authorized by the pre-emptor. So how can the analogy be correct? Similarly in invalid sale, the buyer was authorized by the seller to dispose, unlike the buyer and pre-emptor. Why the restriction "according to Abu Hanifa"? Because the absence of the right of return in invalid sale is the statement of Imam Abu Hanifa. Otherwise, the two companions say that in an invalid sale, when the buyer has built a house on the purchased property, the seller has the right to take it back.
In any case, analogizing pre-emption to these two is not correct due to the above reason, and because the right of taking back in gift and invalid sale is weak, while in pre-emption, taking back from the buyer is valid in any case. In any case, after building, the right of return does not remain in gift and invalid sale, but it remains in pre-emption, so when the pre-emptor's right of recovery exists, what is the meaning of imposing a price on him?
And understand it like this: Someone bought a plot and built a house on it, and then someone claimed that he is the owner of this land. The judge decided in favor of the rightful owner, so the rightful owner will take the land and have the buyer's building demolished, and no guarantee or price will be obligatory on the rightful owner. However, the buyer has the right to refer to the seller and recover his price for the building from the seller.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute between the Zahir al-Riwayah position (supported by the author) and Imam Abu Yusuf's qiyas. It begins by establishing the foundational principle: any disposition made by the buyer (including sale, gift, or building) is void because the pre-emptor's right is stronger and the buyer was not authorized. The author analogizes to a mortgagor building in mortgaged property, which is likewise void due to the mortgagee's attached right. Then the reply to Abu Yusuf's qiyas is given: he had analogized pre-emption to gift and invalid sale, but the difference is that in those cases the recipient was authorized by the right-holder, whereas the buyer was not. Moreover, the right of return in gift and invalid sale is weak and does not survive after building, whereas the pre-emptor's right remains. The author also clarifies that Abu Hanifa's specific position on invalid sale is that no right of return exists, while the two companions disagree. The passage ends with an illustrative analogy from the law of rightful claim (istihqaq), where a rightful owner takes back land with building without paying compensation.

Key Terms
شفعہ (shuf'ah): Pre-emption right: the right of a co-owner or neighbor to purchase property sold to an outsider, preventing the buyer's dispositions.
شفیع (shafi'): The pre-emptor: the person who holds the right of preemption.
مشتری (mushtari): The buyer who purchases the property subject to pre-emption; his dispositions are void if the shafi' exercises his right.
تسلیط (tasleet): Authorization by the right-holder for someone to dispose of the property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The pre-emptor must pay the value of the building and keep it.
B) The building is demolished without compensation to the buyer.
C) The buyer can keep the building and the pre-emptor gets the land only.
D) The building is valued and the pre-emptor pays half.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the pre-emptor's right overrides any unauthorized buyer dispositions, and the buyer bears the loss of any construction without compensation from the pre-emptor.

Crops vs Construction: Istihsan in Pre-emption
Distinguishing between crops and construction in pre-emption via istihsan

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on crops and construction in pre-emption, apply istihsan to distinguish between them, and state the valuation method for buildings.

Core Idea
Crops remain with rent by istihsan because they have a known end; buildings must be removed or taken at dismantled value.

The pre-emptor has the right to take back property sold to a third party, but what happens to additions made by the buyer? This passage addresses a dispute within the Hanafi school: Imam Abu Yusuf analogized crops to buildings, but the relied-upon position (Zahir al-Riwayah) distinguishes them. By understanding the role of istihsan (juristic preference), you will see why crops are treated leniently while buildings are not.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling context
The passage deals with pre-emption (shuf'ah): when a pre-emptor reclaims property from a buyer, what are the options regarding crops and construction added by the buyer?
2
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute
Imam Abu Yusuf argued by qiyas that crops should be treated like buildings (both must be removed). The author responds by distinguishing them.
Abu Yusuf's qiyas is the opposing position; Zahir al-Riwayah is the preferred view.
3
Understand the qiyas vs istihsan reasoning
Strict qiyas would remove crops, but istihsan keeps them because crops have a known end (harvest) and can remain with rent, causing minimal harm to the pre-emptor.
Istihsan overrides qiyas when the latter leads to hardship.
4
Apply the distinction to crops and construction
Buildings and gardens have no fixed end; removing or valuing them as standing would cause significant harm. Hence they must be removed or taken at dismantled value.
The 'known end' (nihayah ma'lumah) is the key illa (effective cause) for the distinction.
5
Derive the valuation rule for buildings
If the pre-emptor chooses to pay for the building, the value is assessed as if dismantled, not as standing property. This follows the rule from the book of Ghasp (usurpation).
This prevents the pre-emptor from being forced to pay for added value he did not consent to.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
امام ابو یوسف کے دوسرے قیاس کا جواب
والزرع يقلع قياسا وانما لا يقلع استحسانا لان له نهاية معلومة ويبقى بالاجر وليس فيه كثير ضرر
ترجمہ اور کھیتی اکھاڑی جائے گی باعتبار قیاس کے اور استحسانا نہیں اکھاڑی جائے گی۔ اسلئے کہ کھیتی کی نہایت معلوم ہے اور وہ باقی رہے گی اجرت کے ساتھ اور اس میں زیادہ ضرر نہیں ہے۔
تشریح امام ابو یوسف نے شفعہ کو کھیتی پر قیاس کیا تھا تو اس کا جواب دیا کہ یہ قیاس صحیح نہیں ہے۔ کیونکہ کھیتی کے سلسلہ میں قیاس تو یہ کہتا ہے کہ اکھاڑ کی جائے گی اور استحسان کا تقاضہ یہ ہے کہ نہیں اکھاڑی جائے گی۔ اس لئے کہ کھیتی کی مدت معلوم ہے۔ اور تعمیر اور باغات کی نہایت معلوم نہیں تو جب معلوم ہے کہ کھیتی تو مثلاً ایک مہینہ کے بعد کاٹ لی جائے گی اور مشتری سے ایک ماہ کی اجرت وکرامیہ لیا جا سکتا ہے تو اس میں شفیع کا کچھ زیادہ نقصان نہیں ہے تو بہر حال تعمیر اور باغ کوکھیتی پر قیاس کرتا صیح نہیں ہے۔
مکان اور پودے کی کون سی قیمت کا اعتبار ہے
وان اخذه بالقيمة يعتبر قيمته مقلوعا كما بيناه فيا الغصب
ترجمہ اور اگر شفیع نے لیا اس کو ( تعمیر کو ) قیمت کے بدلے تو اس کی اکھڑی ہوئی کی قیمت کا اعتبار ہوگا۔ جیسا کہ ہم کتاب الغصب میں بیان کر چکے ہیں۔
تشریح اگر شفیع نے یہ اختیار کر لیا کہ میں اس کی تعمیر یا پودے کی قیمت دے دوں گا تو فرماتے ہیں کہ کھڑے مکان اور پودے کی قیمت کا اعتبار نہ ہوگا بلکہ اگر یہ عمارت اکھاڑ دی جائے اور یہ پودا اکھاڑ دیا جائے اس وقت اس کی جو قیمت ہوگی شفیع اتنی قیمت ادا کرے گا۔
Reply to the second qiyas of Imam Abu Yusuf:
"And the crop is removed by qiyas, but it is not removed by istihsan because it has a known end and remains with rent, and there is not much harm in it."
Translation: And the crop will be removed by qiyas, but by istihsan it will not be removed. Because the crop has a known end, and it will remain with rent, and there is not much harm in it.
Explanation: Imam Abu Yusuf had analogized pre-emption to crops. So the reply is that this qiyas is not correct. Because regarding crops, qiyas says it should be removed, but istihsan requires that it not be removed. Because the duration of the crop is known. But the end of construction and gardens is not known. When it is known that the crop will be harvested after, say, one month, and one month's rent can be taken from the buyer, then there is not much loss to the pre-emptor. So in any case, analogizing construction and gardens to crops is not correct.
"And if he takes it with price, its value is considered as dismantled, as we explained in the book of Ghasp."
Translation: And if the pre-emptor takes it (the construction) with price, then its value as dismantled will be considered, as we have explained in the book of Ghasp.
Explanation: If the pre-emptor chooses to give the price for the construction or trees, it is said that the value of the standing building and trees will not be considered. Rather, if this building is dismantled and this tree is uprooted, the value at that time will be what the pre-emptor pays.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage addresses Abu Yusuf's second qiyas, where he analogized the pre-emptor's case to that of crops planted by a buyer. The author explains that by strict qiyas (analogy), crops would be removed, but by istihsan (juristic preference), they are not removed because they have a known end (e.g., harvest time) and can be left in place with the buyer paying rent. This causes minimal harm to the pre-emptor. In contrast, buildings and gardens have no fixed end and cannot be easily valued or left standing without significant harm, so they must be removed. The passage also addresses valuation: if the pre-emptor opts to pay for the building or trees, the value is assessed as if dismantled (not as standing property), as established in the Book of Ghasp (usurpation).

Key Terms
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference: departing from strict qiyas to avoid hardship, used here to allow crops to remain with rent.
قیاس (qiyas): Analogical reasoning: here, the strict analogy would require crops to be removed like buildings.
غصب (ghasp): Usurpation: the book of fiqh dealing with wrongful taking of property; valuation of buildings is borrowed from that context.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling in this passage, why are crops not removed when the pre-emptor takes the property?
A) Because crops are owned by the buyer and cannot be transferred.
B) Because crops have a known end and can remain with rent, causing little harm.
C) Because the pre-emptor does not have a right over crops.
D) Because the buyer must remove them before the pre-emptor takes over.

Key Takeaway
Istihsan overrides strict qiyas when the object has a known end and minimal harm; buildings and permanent additions are treated strictly.

Pre-emptor's Building: Forced Possessor Not Entitled to Compensation
Intra-Hanafi dispute on pre-emptor's right to building value

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school distinguishes between a pre-emptor and a deceived buyer regarding building value recovery, and evaluate the position of Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
A pre-emptor's building on claimed land is a loss he bears alone—he is not deceived or authorized, only forced.

This passage tackles a common scenario: a pre-emptor builds or plants on land he acquires through shuf'ah, only to have a third party later prove rightful ownership. Does the pre-emptor recover the value of his improvements? The Hanafi school draws a sharp contrast between a pre-emptor (who exercises a forced right) and an ordinary buyer (who acts voluntarily and may be deceived). Understanding this distinction reveals foundational principles of authorization (tasleet) and deception (gharar) in property law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and default ruling
Read the first part of the passage: the pre-emptor takes land, builds/plants, then the land is claimed (istihqaaq). The default ruling is that he recovers only the price, not the value of additions.
The ruling applies whether the pre-emptor took the land from the seller or from the original buyer.
2
Examine Abu Yusuf's dissenting view
The passage notes that Abu Yusuf allows recovery of building value. His rationale: the pre-emptor is considered a possessor in the position of an owner (mutamallak), making the seller or buyer liable as if they were the seller of the land with improvements.
Abu Yusuf's reasoning analogizes the pre-emptor to a deceived buyer (maghroor).
3
Contrast with the well-known (mashhur) position
The well-known position rejects Abu Yusuf's analogy. The key distinction: a buyer is deceived by the seller and is authorized to possess, whereas the pre-emptor is forced (majboor) and has no relationship of authorization with the seller/buyer.
Without tasleet (authorization) or gharar (deception), the pre-emptor bears the loss of his own voluntary additions.
4
Derive the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: under Abu Yusuf, the pre-emptor would claim compensation from the seller or buyer; under the mashhur, he recovers only the purchase price, absorbing the loss of his improvements.
This difference affects risk allocation in property transactions involving pre-emption.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو اخذها الشفيع فيني فيها اوغرس ثم استحقت رجع بالثمن لانه تبين انه اخذه بغير حق ولا يرجع بقيمة البناء والغرس لا على البائع ان اخذها منه ولا على المشترى ان اخذها منه وعن ابى يوسف انه يرجع لانه متملك عليه فنزلا منزلة البائع والمشترى والفرق على ما هو المشهور ان المشترى مغرور من جهة البائع ومسلط عليه من جهة ولا غرور ولا تسليط فى حق الشفيع من المشترى لانه مجبور عليــــه
And if the pre-emptor takes it (the land) and builds on it or plants, then it is claimed by a rightful owner, he recovers the price (he paid) because it is clear that he took it without right. And he does not recover the value of the building and planting, neither from the seller if he took it from him, nor from the buyer if he took it from him. And it is reported from Abu Yusuf that he recovers, because he is considered a possessor in the position of a buyer, so they (the seller and buyer) are considered like a seller and buyer. And the difference according to what is well-known is that the buyer is deceived by the seller and authorized against him, but there is no deception or authorization in the right of the pre-emptor from the buyer, because he is forced upon him.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage states the ruling when a pre-emptor takes possession of the property, builds or plants, and then the land is claimed by a rightful owner (e.g., a third party proves ownership). The pre-emptor gets back the price he paid (for the land) from the person from whom he took it (seller or original buyer), but he does not recover the value of his building or trees. This is because his possession was wrongful in hindsight (the land was not rightfully sold). The pre-emptor is not considered deceived or authorized by the seller or buyer; rather, he is compelled to take the property (the pre-emption right is forced). Abu Yusuf holds a different view: he says the pre-emptor does recover building value because he is like a buyer who was deceived. But the well-known position (mashhur) is that the pre-emptor is not entitled to compensation for his additions.

Key Terms
متملك (mutamallak): Possessor in the position of an owner; Abu Yusuf treats the pre-emptor as such to allow recovery of building value.
مغرور (maghroor): Deceived person; the buyer is deceived by the seller, but the pre-emptor is not.
استحقاق (istihqaaq): Legal claim by a rightful owner; when proven, prior possessions are invalidated.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the well-known (mashhur) Hanafi position, if a pre-emptor builds on the land and then a rightful owner emerges, what does the pre-emptor recover?
A) The price he paid plus the value of the building.
B) Only the price he paid for the land.
C) Only the value of the building.
D) Nothing at all.

Key Takeaway
The pre-emptor bears the loss of his buildings because he is forced, not deceived; compensation only covers the purchase price.

Qiyas vs Istihsan: Buildings and Crops in Shuf'a
Explains that buildings/trees are uprooted by qiyas but farming remains by istihsan, and compensation is as dismantled.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school distinguishes between farming and buildings in shuf'a, applying qiyas and istihsan.

Core Idea
Farming stays by istihsan; buildings are uprooted by qiyas — the known end of crops reduces harm.

In this passage from Al-Hidayah, al-Marghinani explains the rule for crops and structures on land taken by pre-emption. The default qiyas requires uprooting everything, but istihsan spares farming because its harm is limited. Understanding this distinction is key to applying the Hanafi method of conflict resolution between qiyas and istihsan.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objects and their rulings
The passage addresses buildings/trees and farming (zira'a). State the ruling for each: buildings/trees can be uprooted or compensated; farming remains with rent.
Farming is not uprooted by default; it is an exception.
2
Locate the legal basis (dalil)
Identify the sources: qiyas for uprooting, istihsan for farming. The passage invokes qiyas and istihsan without quoting specific texts.
Istihsan overrides qiyas here.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah (reasoning)
Explain why istihsan applies to farming but not to buildings: farming has a known end (nihaaya ma'luma) and can be rented, causing less harm; buildings have no known end and cause greater harm.
The 'known end' is the key differentiator.
4
Determine the compensation rule
If the shafi' chooses compensation instead of uprooting, the value is assessed as dismantled (qimat maqlu'), not as standing.
This mirrors the rule in ghasp (usurpation).
5
Identify the mufta bihi (operative position)
Al-Marghinani favors istihsan for farming. The default qiyas for buildings stands, but the shafi' has the option to pay compensation.
The mufta bihi is the istihsan-based ruling for farming.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والزرع يقلع قياسا وانما لا يقلع استحسانا لان له نهاية معلومة ويبقى بالاجر وليس فيه كثير ضرر وان اخذه بالقيمة يعتبر قيمته مقلوعا كما بيناه فيا الغصب
And the crop is uprooted by analogy, but it is not uprooted by juristic preference because it has a known end and remains with rent and there is not much harm in it. And if he takes it with compensation, its value is considered as dismantled, as we explained in the chapter of usurpation.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof. The text from Al-Hidayah addresses the status of constructions and trees on land subject to pre-emption (shuf'a). By default, qiyas (analogical reasoning) requires that any structure or tree be uprooted so the pre-emptor (shafi') can take the land barren. However, farming (zira'a) is treated differently by istihsan (juristic preference): because farming has a known harvest date, the shafi' can let it stay and take rent instead. For buildings and trees, there is no known end, so the qiyas of uprooting stands, unless the shafi' chooses to pay compensation. If he does, the compensation is based on the value of the structure as if dismantled (qimat maqlu'), not as standing. This is analogous to the rules in the chapter on usurpation (ghasp).

Key Terms
شفعة (shuf'a): The right of a co-owner or neighbor to purchase a shared property by pre-emption when sold to an outsider.
قياس (qiyas): Analogous reasoning from an original case (asl) to a new case (far') due to shared effective cause (illa).
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference that overrides a qiyas ruling due to a stronger legal consideration.
قيمة مقلوع (qimat maqlu'): The value of a structure or tree after it has been dismantled or uprooted, not as standing.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is farming on pre-emptible land not uprooted like buildings?
A) Because farming is more important to the economy.
B) Because farming has a known end and can be rented, causing less harm.
C) Because the shafi' cannot demand removal of crops under any circumstance.
D) Because qiyas requires it to stay.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi shuf'a, crops stay by istihsan due to known end; buildings are uprooted by qiyas unless compensated.

Shafi' Builds on Land Then Istiḥqāq: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Ruling on whether the shafi' can recover building value after the land is claimed, with a view from Abu Yusuf.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on whether a shafi' recovers the value of improvements after istiḥqāq, distinguish Abu Yusuf's position, and apply the concepts of ghurur and taslit.

Core Idea
If the shafi' builds on pre-empted land later claimed, he recovers only the price, not improvements, because the prior buyer was compelled and not deceptive.

In shuf'a (pre-emption), the shafi' steps into the shoes of the buyer. But what happens if he makes improvements and then the land is claimed by a rightful owner? Does he recover his investment? This passage examines an intra-Hanafi dispute where Abu Yusuf differs from the majority, and explains the underlying legal reasoning based on the absence of deception (ghurur) and authorization (taslit). Understanding this distinction is crucial for a shafi' who may contemplate improving the property before the risk of istiḥqāq is resolved.

Guided Path
1
Identify the case and the initial ruling
Read the arabic passage: 'Wa law akhadhaha al-shafi'...' The text presents a scenario: the shafi' takes the land, builds or plants, then the land is claimed (istiḥqāq). The initial ruling states that the shafi' recovers only the price, not the value of improvements.
The price is the amount he paid to the seller or prior buyer.
2
Identify the majority Hanafi position and its rationale
The majority (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) hold that the shafi' does not recover the value of buildings or trees. The reason given: 'it became clear he took it without right' (tabayyana annahu akhadhahu bi ghayri haqq). Additionally, there is no deception (ghurur) or authorization (taslit) from the prior buyer because the prior buyer was compelled (majboor) to hand over the land.
Key terms: ghurur, taslit, majboor.
3
Identify Abu Yusuf's opposing position and its rationale
Abu Yusuf holds that the shafi' can recover the value of improvements. His reasoning: the shafi' is a buyer from the prior buyer, and the prior buyer is like a seller. Therefore, the shafi' is entitled to compensation for losses just as a deceived buyer would be.
Abu Yusuf treats the shafi' as a deceived buyer (mamallak).
4
Analyse the difference: the key concepts of ghurur and taslit
The text distinguishes: in a normal sale, the buyer is deceived by the seller (ghurur) and is authorized by the seller to use the property (taslit). In shuf'a, the prior buyer is compelled to sell; he did not voluntarily authorize the shafi' nor deceive him. Therefore, the rule of ghurur does not apply, and the shafi' bears the risk of improvements.
Ghurur and taslit are absent in the shafi' relationship.
5
Determine the mufta bihi (operative position)
The well-known (mashhoor) position, which is the mufta bihi, is that of Abu Hanifa: the shafi' does not recover building value. This is the ruling relied upon in fatwa.
Tarjih authority: Al-Marghinani in Al-Hidayah states this as the mashhoor.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو اخذها الشفيع فبنى فيها او غرس ثم استحقت رجع بالثمن لانه تبين انه اخذه بغير حق ولا يرجع بقيمة البناء والغرس لا على البائع ان اخذها منه ولا على المشترى ان اخذها منه وعن ابى يوسف انه يرجع لانه متملك عليه فنزلا منزلة البائع والمشترى والفرق على ما هو المشهور ان المشترى مغرور من جهة البائع ومسلط عليه من جهة ولا غرور ولا تسليط فى حق الشفيع من المشترى لانه مجبور عليه
And if the shafi' takes it (the land) and builds in it or plants, then it is claimed (by a rightful owner), he recovers the price because it became clear that he took it without right. And he does not recover the value of the building and planting from the seller if he took it from him, nor from the buyer if he took it from him. And from Abu Yusuf that he does recover, because he has ownership over him, so they (the seller and buyer) are treated as the seller and buyer (in a sale). And the difference according to what is well known is that the buyer is deceived from the seller's side and is authorized by him, while there is no deception nor authorization in the right of the shafi' from the buyer, because he (the buyer) was compelled (to give the land to the shafi').
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute. The text considers a case: the shafi' exercises his right of pre-emption and takes possession of the land, then builds a house or plants trees. Subsequently, a third party proves rightful ownership (istiḥqāq), and the land is taken from the shafi'. The ruling of the school (Abu Hanifa) is that the shafi' can only recover the price he paid (al-thaman) from the seller or the prior buyer (depending on whom he took it from). He cannot recover the value of his building or trees. The reason: it became clear that the shafi' took the land without right (i.e., the seller/prior buyer did not actually own it). But Abu Yusuf holds that the shafi' can recover the value as well, because the seller/prior buyer is now considered a seller to the shafi' (since he transferred ownership), and the shafi' is a buyer who was misled. The text then explains the difference: in a normal sale, the buyer (al-mushtari) is deceived by the seller and is authorized by the seller to use the property. But the shafi' is not deceived by the prior buyer; rather, the prior buyer was compelled (majboor) to hand over the land to the shafi' due to the right of pre-emption. Therefore, there is no element of deception (ghurur) or authorization (taslit) from the prior buyer's side, so the shafi' cannot claim compensation from him. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is that of Abu Hanifa and Imam Muhammad (the majority) that the shafi' does not recover building value.

Key Terms
استحقاق (istiḥqāq): The emergence of a rightful claimant to the property, proving that the previous owner did not have valid title.
غرور (ghurur): Deception or misleading another party into a transaction or action.
تسليط (taslit): Authorization or empowerment of another to use property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi school (Abu Hanifa) deny the shafi' compensation for buildings when the land is later claimed?
A) Because the shafi' was negligent.
B) Because the prior buyer was compelled and did not deceive the shafi'.
C) Because the building is considered a gift.
D) Because the shafi' cannot claim against the rightful owner.

Key Takeaway
In shuf'a, the shafi' bears the risk of improvements if the land is later claimed, because the prior buyer was compelled and not deceiving; the mufta bihi is that no compensation is due for buildings or trees.

Shafi's Option When Accessories Are Destroyed Naturally
Ruling from Quduri on shafi' options in case of accidental destruction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the shafi's two options when buildings or trees on pre-emptible property are destroyed without human action, and explain why the price remains unchanged.

Core Idea
If the building burns or trees die naturally, the shafi' pays full price because they were accessories (tabi').

This passage from al-Quduri's Mukhtasar lays out a clear ruling in shuf'a. When property subject to pre-emption suffers accidental destruction of its structures or trees, the shafi' faces a choice: take the land at the full price or walk away. Understanding this ruling requires grasping the Hanafi concept of tabi' and how it affects pricing.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The passage applies when the building collapses, burns, or trees dry up without human action (e.g., natural disaster).
2
State the shafi's two options
He may take the land with full price or abandon the claim.
These are the only two options.
3
Understand the reasoning for full price
Buildings and trees are tabi' (accessories); no part of the price was allocated to them, so their destruction doesn't reduce the price.
Contrast with loss of the land itself.
4
Contrast with loss of land itself
If part of the land is lost (e.g., submerged), the shafi' takes the remainder with a proportional share of the price, because the lost part is part of the asl.
This exception shows the rule's boundary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال واذا انهدمت الدار واحترق بناؤها او جف شجر البستان بغير فعل احد فالشفيع بالخيار ان شاء اخذها بجميع الثمن لان البناء والغرس تابع حتى دخلا فى البيع من غير ذكر فلا يقابلهما شئ من الثمن مالم يصر مقصودا ولهذا يبيعها مرابحة بكل الثمن في هذه الصورة بخلاف ما اذا غرق نصف الارض حيث ياخذ الباقي بحصته لان الفائت بعض الاصل قال وان شاء ترك لان له ان يمتنع عن تملك الدار بماله
He (al-Quduri) said: And if the house collapses and its building burns or the trees of the garden dry up without anyone's action, then the shafi' has the option: if he wishes, he takes it with the full price, because the building and trees are accessories (tabi') that entered the sale without mention, so nothing of the price corresponds to them unless they become intended; for this reason he sells it as a profit sale with the full price in this case, unlike when half of the land is submerged where he takes the remainder with its share because the lost part is part of the original. And if he wishes, he can abandon (the claim) because he has the right to refrain from owning the house with his money.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage from Al-Quduri's Mukhtasar is a ruling statement. It addresses the situation where the pre-emptible property's building or trees are destroyed by natural causes (fire, collapse, drought) without human interference. The shafi' has two options: he may still take the land, paying the full price (al-thaman) as agreed in the original sale, or he may abandon his claim to pre-emption. The reason the full price is paid is that buildings and trees are considered accessories (tabi') to the land; in the original sale, no specific portion of the price was allocated to them unless they were the main object (which they are not). Therefore, even if they are destroyed, the price remains the same. This is contrasted with a case where part of the land itself is lost (e.g., half submerged), in which case the shafi' takes only the remaining land with a proportional share of the price. The shafi' may also simply withdraw from the claim since he is not forced to take the land. This ruling is a clear matn for memorization.

Key Terms
تابع (tabi'): An accessory or appurtenance that is part of the property but not separately priced in the sale.
خيار (khiyar): Option or choice given to a party to a contract or legal proceeding.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Quduri, if the building on pre-emptible land burns down naturally, what are the shafi'’s options?
A) He must take the land and pay full price.
B) He may take the land for full price or abandon the claim.
C) He may take the land at a reduced price.
D) He must wait until the building is rebuilt.

Key Takeaway
The shafi' has the right to take the land at the full price or leave it, because the destroyed accessories were never assigned a portion of the price.

Shafi's Building on Reclaimed Land: Only Price, Not Compensation
Intra-Hanafi dispute on shafi's building compensation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the shafi' who builds on preempted land later reclaimed, and explain why Imam Abu Yusuf's differing view is not the mufta bihi.

Core Idea
The shafi' takes land without full right; his building is at his own risk, so he gets only the price paid.

A shafi' exercises pre-emption, takes land, and builds a house. Later, the true owner reclaims the land. What recourse does the shafi' have? The standard Hanafi ruling holds that he only recovers the purchase price (thaman) and must remove his building at his own cost. But Imam Abu Yusuf argues that the shafi' can recover losses from the seller or buyer, drawing analogy to a buyer whose purchase is reclaimed. This lesson unpacks the reasoning behind the standard view and the distinction that prevents analogy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the legal question
Understand the facts: shafi' takes preempted land, builds a house/plants. Then a third party proves rightful ownership. The key question: can the shafi' recover his building costs?
The property is reclaimed from the shafi', not from the original seller or buyer.
2
State the standard Hanafi position
The shafi' only gets back the purchase price (from the person he paid) and must remove the building at his own cost. He gets nothing for the building from anyone.
The rationale: it became clear he took the land without right.
3
Identify the opposing view of Imam Abu Yusuf
Imam Abu Yusuf holds that the shafi' can recover the loss (value of building or damage) from the seller or buyer, just as a buyer whose property is reclaimed recovers from the seller.
Abu Yusuf uses qiyas: the shafi' is like a deceived buyer.
4
Analyze the distinction given by the author
The author distinguishes: In the buyer case, the seller deceived the buyer and entrusted him. In the shafi' case, the buyer did not deceive the shafi'; rather, the buyer was compelled to sell by pre-emption.
Thus, the deception (taghrir) is missing in the shafi' case, so no recourse.
5
Determine the operative ruling (mufta bihi)
The standard view of Abu Hanifa is the relied-upon ruling. Abu Yusuf's view is a minority opinion within the school.
Practical consequence: shafi' absorbs the loss of building unless the seller/buyer was fraudulent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مالکہ ہوا ہے اس پر (جس سے لیا ہے ) تو اتار لئے جائیں گے یہ دونوں بائع اور مشتری کے درجہ میں ۔ اور فرق اس روایت کے مطابق جو مشہور ہے یہ ہے کہ مشتری کو دھوکہ دیا گیا ہے بائع کی جانب سے اور مسلط ہوا ہے اس پر بائع کی جانب سے اور غرور و تسلیط نہیں ہے شفیع کے حق میں مشتری کی جانب سے اسلئے کہ مشتری مجبور ہے اس پر ( شفعہ دینے پر )۔ تشریح زمین مشفوع کو شفیع نے لے کر اس میں مکان بنادیا یا باغ لگا دیا اب کسی نے اس زمین کے استحقاق کا دعوی کر دیا اور قاضی نے مستحق کیلئے فیصلہ کر دیا تو زمین متحق کی گئی۔
اور شفیع کو کہا جائے گا کہ اپنی عمارت یا پودے اکھاڑ کر لے جا عمارت کی قیمت یا اس کے ٹوٹنے کا نقصان اس کو نہیں ملے گا۔ بلکہ اس کو صرف شمن ملے گا جو بائع سے لینے کی صورت میں بائع ادا کرے گا۔ اور مشتری سے لینے کی صورت میں مشتری ادا کرے گا اور شفیع کو تم وغیرہ کی قیمت نہ مستحق سے ملے گی اور نہ بائع سے نہ مشتری سے۔
سوال اس کو فقط شمن ہی کیوں ملے گا اور قیمت بناء کیوں نہیں ملے گی ؟
جواب ..... لانه تبين انه اخذہ بغیر حق اسلئے کہ یہ بات واضح ہو گئی کہ شفیع نے اس کو بغیر حق کے لیا ہے۔ امام ابو یوسف فرماتے ہیں کہ شفیع نقصان کی تلافی بائع یا مشتری سے کرے گا یعنی بائع سے لینے کی صورت میں نقصان بائع سے وصول کرے گا اور مشتری سے لینے کی صورت میں نقصان مشترکی سے بھول کرے گا کیونکہ شفیع نے ان دونوں میں کسی ایک سے ملکیت حاصل کی ہے۔
تو شفیع کو اور جس سے شفیع نے لیا ہے ان دونوں کو بائع اور مشتری کے درجہ میں اتار لیا جائے گا اور مشتری استحقاق بیع کی صورت میں بائع کی جانب رجوع کرتا ہے تو ایسے ہی شفیع بھی رجوع کرے گا۔ پھر مصنف نے ان دونوں کے درمیان فرق بتلایا کہ بائع اور مشتری والے مسئلہ میں بائع نے مشتری کو دھوکہ دیا ہے اور بائع نے ہی مشتری کو اس پر تعریف کرنے کیلئے مسلط کیا ہے اس وجہ سے بائع کی جانب رجوع کیا گیا۔ مگر شفیع والے مسئلہ میں شفیع کومشتری نے کچھ دھو کہ نہیں دیا اور نہ مشتری نے شفیع کو مسلط کیا بلکہ مشتری نے تو مجوسی ہو کر یہ زمین شفیع کے نوالہ کی ہے اس وجہ سے اول صورت میں رجوع صحیح اور ثانی میں صحیح نہیں ہے۔
He became owner of it; from (whom he took) then both will be placed in the position of seller and buyer. And the difference according to the well-known narration is that the buyer was deceived by the seller and was entrusted by the seller, but there is no deception or entrustment towards the shafi' from the buyer because the buyer is compelled (to give pre-emption). Explanation: The shafi' took the preempted land and built a house or planted a garden on it. Then someone claimed rightful ownership of the land and the judge decided in favor of the rightful owner, so the land was given to him. And the shafi' will be told to remove his building or plants; he will not be given the value of the building or the loss from its destruction. Rather, he will only get the price (thaman), which in case he took from the seller, the seller will pay, and in case he took from the buyer, the buyer will pay. The shafi' will not get the price of the building etc. from the rightful owner, nor from the seller, nor from the buyer. Question: Why does he only get the price and not the value of the building? Answer: ... because it became clear that he took it without right. Imam Abu Yusuf says that the shafi' will recover the loss from the seller or buyer, i.e., if he took from the seller, he will recover the loss from the seller, and if he took from the buyer, he will recover the loss from the buyer, because the shafi' acquired ownership from one of these two. So the shafi' and the one from whom he took are both placed in the position of seller and buyer. And in the case of a buyer whose property is reclaimed, the buyer reverts to the seller; similarly the shafi' will revert. Then the author explained the difference between the two cases: in the seller-buyer case, the seller deceived the buyer and entrusted him to praise the property, so reversion is made to the seller. But in the shafi' case, the buyer did not deceive the shafi' nor entrust him; rather, the buyer was compelled to sell the land to the shafi'. Therefore, in the first case reversion is valid, but in the second it is not.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the case where a shafi' (pre-emptor) takes land through shuf'ah, builds a house or plants trees, and then the true owner reclaims the land. The ruling: the shafi' must remove his building/plants at his own cost; he receives only the purchase price (thaman) from the person from whom he took the land (seller or buyer). The reason: it became clear he took without right. Imam Abu Yusuf differs, holding that the shafi' can recover his loss from the seller or buyer by analogy to the buyer in a case of istihqaq. The author distinguishes the two cases: in the buyer's case, the seller deceived him, so recourse is to the seller; in the shafi's case, the buyer did not deceive, so no recourse. The standard Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa) is that the shafi' only gets the price.

Key Terms
شفعة (shuf'ah): The right of pre-emption: a property owner's right to purchase a co-owned or adjacent property when it is sold to an outsider.
شفیع (shafi'): The person entitled to exercise the right of pre-emption (shuf'ah).
ثمن (thaman): The purchase price paid for the property.
مستحق (mustahiq): The true rightful owner who reclaims the property.
تغریر (taghrir): Deception or inducement that gives the deceived party a right of recourse.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the standard Hanafi ruling, why does the shafi' not recover the value of a building he constructed on preempted land that is later reclaimed?
A) Because the shafi' intentionally built on land he knew was disputed.
B) Because it became clear he took the land without right, and there was no deception (taghrir) by the person he paid.
C) Because the building belongs to the true owner automatically.
D) Because the shafi' had no permission from the true owner.

Key Takeaway
A shafi' who builds on preempted land later reclaimed by the true owner receives only the purchase price, not compensation for improvements, because his acquisition is deemed without right and no deception occurred.

Shafi's Option When Preempted Property Destroyed by Natural Causes
Ruling on shafi's option when preempted property destroyed by natural causes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the shafi's two options when the preempted property is destroyed by natural causes, apply the Hanafi principle of tabi' to determine whether the price is reduced, and distinguish between loss of subordinate parts (tabi') and loss of the original (asl).

Core Idea
Buildings and trees are subordinate to land; their loss does not reduce the pre-emption price — only loss of the land itself does.

This passage from Al-Quduri addresses a critical nuance: when the preempted property suffers damage before the shafi' takes possession. The ruling turns on the distinction between what is subordinate (tabi') and what is the original (asl). Understanding this distinction is essential for applying the law correctly in shuf'ah cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario
Recognize that the property (house or garden) is destroyed by a natural cause (not human action).
The text specifies: house collapses, building burns, garden trees dry up without anyone's action.
2
State the Shafi's Two Options
The shafi' has a choice (khiyar): he may take the land for the full price, or he may abandon his right of pre-emption.
He is not compelled to take damaged property.
3
Understand the Ratio Legis: Tabi'
Buildings and trees are subordinate (tabi') to the land. They are included in the sale without mention, so nothing of the price is allocated to them. Therefore, their destruction does not affect the price.
This principle is derived from the fact that in a normal sale, buildings/trees pass without additional price.
4
Contrast with Loss of the Original (Asl)
If part of the land itself is lost (e.g., half the plot is eroded by a river), the shafi' takes the remaining portion at a proportionate price because the loss is to the original of the property.
This contrast clarifies the boundary of the tabi' rule.
5
Apply the Principle to the Murabahah Example
The shafi' can resell the land by murabahah (cost-plus) for the full price without disclosing the destroyed buildings/trees, confirming that the price is unchanged.
This is a practical proof that buildings/trees are not considered separate elements.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
گھر منہدم ہو گیا، عمارت جل گئی ، باغ کے درخت جل گئے بغیر کسی کے فعل کے تو شفیع کیلئے کیا حکم ہے
قال واذا انهدمت الدار واحترق بناؤها اوجف شجر البستان بغير فعل احد فالشفيع بالخيار ان شا اخذها يجميع الثمن لان البنا و الغرس تابع حتى دخلا فى البيع من غير ذكر فلا يقابلهما شئ من الثمن مالم يصر مقصودا ولهذا يبيعها مرابحة بكل الثمن في هذه الصورة بخلاف ما اذا غرق نصف الارض حيث ياخذ الباقي بحصته لان الفائت بعض الاصل قال وان شأترك لان له ان يمتنع عن تملك الدار بماله
ترجمہ اہم قدوری نے کہا اور جب گھر منہدم ہو گیا یا اس کی تعمیر جل گئی یا باغ کے درخت جل گئے بغیر کسی کے فعل کے تو شفیع کو اختیار ہے اگر چاہے اس کو لے لے پورے ثمن کے ساتھ ۔ اسلئے کہ بناء اور غرس تابع ہیں یہاں تک کہ بغیر ذکر کے بیج میں داخل ہوتے ہیں تو اند
کے مقابلہ میں کچھ شمن نہیں آئے گا۔ جب تک کہ مقصود نہ ہو اور اسی وجہ سے وہ اس کو اس صورت میں پورے شمن کے ساتھ بیع مرابحہ کے طور پر بچ سکتا ہے بخلاف اس صورت کے جبکہ زمین کا نصف حصہ غرق ہو جائے اس حیثیت سے کہ شفیع باقی کو اس کے حصہ کے بدلے لے سکتا ہے۔ اسلئے کہ جو فوت ہوا ہے وہ اصل کا بعض حصہ لے قدوری نے کہا اور اگر چاہے چھوڑ دے اسلئے کہ شفیع کو حق ہے کہ وہ رک جائے اپنے مال کے بدلے گھر کا مالک ہونے سے۔
تشریح یہ بات تو آپ کو پہلے سے معلوم ہے کہ وصف موصوف کے تابع ہے اور یہ اصول مقرر ہے کہ جب تک وصف مقصود نہ بنے تب تک وصف کے مقابلہ میں ثمن نہیں آئے گا۔ یعنی وصف کے اضافہ سے شمن میں اضافہ نہ ہوگا۔ اور وصف کے گھٹ جانے سے شمن میں کمی نہ ہوگی۔
اور تعمیر میں آگ لگ کر اس کا جل جانا اور عمارت کا گر جانا یا باغ کے درختوں کا خشک ہو جانا یہ دار اور باغ کے اوصاف ہیں کیونکہ یہ دار اور باغ کے تابع ہیں۔ ماور تمیں بیگہ زمین میں سے پندرہ کا ڈوب جانا یعنی دریا میں مل جانا یہ وصف کی کمی نہیں بلکہ اصل اور ذات کی کمی ہے کیونکہ ذات ارض میں کمی واقع ہوگئی۔ بہر حال اول تابع اور دوسری صورت میں پندرہ بیکہ زمین دوسری پندرہ کے تابع نہیں ہے۔ اول کے وصف ہونے کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ گر کسی نے دار خریدا اور عمارت کا اس میں کوئی ذکر نہیں آیا تب بھی عمارت بیچ میں داخل ہو گی ۔ ایسے یہ زمین کی خریداری میں اس کے درخت اخل ہوں گے معلوم ہوا کہ یہ اوصاف ہیں۔ اب مسئلہ سنئے کہ اگر مشری نے کوئی باغ یا گھر خریدا اور وہ گھر منہدم ہو گیا یا وہ جل گیا یا باغ تھا اسکے درخت خشک ہو گئے اور شفیع نے یہاں شفعہ کا دعویٰ کر رکھا تھا تو اب شفیع کو دو اختیار ہیں: شفعہ کو چھوڑ دے کیونکہ اگر وہ اپنے مال کے بدلہ دار کا مالک نہ بنے تو یہ اس کو حق ہے۔ - اگر لینا چاہتا ہے تو پوراشن ادا کر کے اس مطبع مذکور کو لے سکتا ہے کیونکہ یہ ساری مذکورہ چیزیں اوصاف ہیں اور جب تک اوصاف مقصود نہ ہوں تو اس کی کمی بیشی سے شمن میں کمی بیشی نہیں ہوتی اور شمن میں کمی نہ ہونے کی واضح دلیل یہ ہے کہ اگر شفیع اس زمین کو اسی حالت میں فروخت کرتا ہے یعنی بیچ مرابحہ کرنا چاہے تو بغیر بیان کے پورے شمن کے ساتھ بیچ مرابحہ کر سکتا ہے اور یہ بین ثبوت ہے کہ شمن میں کمی نہ ہوگی ۔ اور اگر زمین کا کچھ حصہ ٹوٹ کر دریا میں مل گیا تو چونکہ یہ اصل میں کی ہوتی ہے اس وجہ سے یہاں شفیع اتنی رقم شمن میں کم کر دے جتنی زمین کم ہوگئی یعنی مثال مذکور میں آدھا من کم کر دیا جائے گا کیونکہ اصل کی کمی ہے۔ لیکن یہ ساری تفصیلات اس وقت میں ہیں جبکہ یہ تغیر اور کی کسی آفت سماویہ کی وجہ سے آئی ہو اس میں کسی کی کارکردگی کا دخل نہ ہو اور اگر کسی کے فعل سے ایسا ہوا ہو تو حکم یہ ہے جو قد وری بیان کر رہے ہیں۔
The house collapsed, the building burnt, the garden trees burnt without anyone's action — what is the ruling for the shafi'? He (al-Quduri) said: When the house collapses, its building burns, or the garden trees dry up without anyone's action, the shafi' has the choice: if he wishes, he may take it for the full price, because the building and plants are subordinate (tabi') — they are included in the sale without mention, so nothing of the price is allocated to them as long as they are not intended. For this reason, he can sell it by murabahah for the full price in this case, unlike when half the land is flooded — then he takes the remainder for its share because what is lost is part of the original. He (al-Quduri) said: And if he wishes, he may leave it, because he has the right to refrain from owning the house with his money. Translation by Quduri: ... (the rest of the commentary explains the principle that attributes are subordinate to the substance).
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from Hanafi fiqh regarding the shafi's (pre-emptor's) rights when the pre-empted property is destroyed by natural causes (such as collapse, fire, drying of trees) before the shafi' takes possession. The ruling is that the shafi' has an option (khiyar): he may either take the land for the full price he agreed to pay, or he may abandon his right of pre-emption. The reason is that buildings and trees are subordinate (tabi') to the land; they are included in the sale without being explicitly mentioned, and therefore their existence or destruction does not affect the price. This principle is contrasted with the case where part of the land itself is lost (e.g., half of the plot is eroded by a river). In that case, the shafi' takes the remaining portion for a proportionate share of the price, because the loss is to the original (asl) of the property, not to a subordinate attribute. The commentary elaborates that buildings and trees are considered attributes (awsaf) of the land; their decrease or increase does not change the price unless they become the primary object of sale. A practical proof: the shafi' can resell the land by murabahah (cost-plus sale) without disclosing the destruction of the buildings/trees, because they are not considered separate elements of the sale. The text notes that this ruling applies only when the destruction is due to a natural disaster (aafat samawiyyah), not if caused by a person's action. If a person caused the destruction, the ruling is different (to be discussed later).

Key Terms
تابع (tabi'): Subordinate or incidental; something that is included in a sale as an accessory without being explicitly priced.
اصل (asl): The original, primary object of the sale, i.e., the land itself.
خيار (khiyar): Option or choice; the shafi' has the right to choose between taking the property or abandoning the pre-emption.
مرابحة (murabahah): A cost-plus sale where the seller discloses the cost and adds a profit margin.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If the pre-empted house collapses by natural cause before the shafi' takes possession, what is the shafi's option?
A) He must take it for full price
B) He can take it for full price or leave it
C) He can take it for reduced price
D) He loses the right of pre-emption

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi shuf'ah, buildings and trees are subordinate (tabi') to the land; their destruction by natural causes does not reduce the pre-emption price — the shafi' may take the land for full price or abandon the right.

Istihsan over Qiyas: Pre-emptor's Right to Attached Fruits
Commentary on pre-emption rule: istihsan vs qiyas for fruit inclusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school applies istihsan over qiyas in granting the shafi' the right to take attached fruits, and distinguish between subordinate (tabi') and non-subordinate property.

Core Idea
Attached fruits are taken by the pre-emptor because physical attachment makes them part of the land, overriding the general rule that movable goods are excluded.

This passage from Al-Hidayah addresses a key debate in the law of pre-emption (shuf'ah): whether the pre-emptor (shafi') takes fruits that are attached to the land at the time of possession. While a strict analogy (qiyas) would exclude fruits as movable property, the Hanafi school adopts istihsan (juristic preference) to include them, based on the legal principle of taba'iyyah (subordination). This lesson walks through the reasoning and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two legal methods
Recognize that the passage contrasts qiyas (analogy) and istihsan (juristic preference). Qiyas would yield one ruling, istihsan another.
Istihsan is a distinctive Hanafi source—it prioritizes equitable outcome over strict analogy.
2
Understand the qiyas argument
Qiyas treats fruits like movable goods (e.g., refrigerator) that are not included in the sale of a house unless mentioned. Since fruits are not mentioned and are separable, they are not subordinate to the land.
The key premise: fruits are not included in the sale without explicit mention.
3
Understand the istihsan argument
Istihsan focuses on physical attachment (ittisal). Fruits are attached to the land like doors or cabinets, making them subordinate (tabi') to the land. Thus, they pass with the land in pre-emption.
The analogy shifts from movable goods to fixed fixtures.
4
Apply the principle of taba'iyyah
Determine whether an item is subordinate by considering if it is physically attached and traditionally treated as part of the property. Unattached goods remain separate.
This principle applies beyond pre-emption, e.g., in sales of real estate with fittings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اس پر صاحب ہدایہ نے فرمایا کہ امام قدوری کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ جب پھلوں کا بیچ میں ذکر آیا ہو کیونکہ بغیر ذکر کے پھل بیچ میں داخل نہیں ہوتے۔
پھر صاحب ہدایہ نے فرمایا کہ جو مسئلہ ذکر کیا گیا ہے یہ بر بناء استحسان ہے ورنہ قیاس کا تقاضہ یہ ہے کہ شفیع کو پھل نہ ملیں کیونکہ پھل درخت کے اور زمین کے تابع نہیں ہیں۔
اور تابع نہ ہونے کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ پھل بغیر ذکر کے بیچ میں داخل نہیں ہوتے تو اس کی مثال ایسی ہوگئی جیسے گھر میں رکھا ہوا سامان،
جیسے کسی نے مکان فروخت کیا اور اس میں فرج اور گیس کا چولہا وغیرہ رکھا ہوا ہے تو یہ بیچ میں داخل نہ ہوں گے۔ استحسان کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ پھل اتصال کے اعتبار سے زمین کے تابع ہے جیسے عمارت گھر کے تابع ہوتی ہے اور ایسے ہی وہ سامان جو گھر
میں لگا دیا جاتا ہے جیسے کواڑ و چوکھٹ اور الماری اور جنگلے وغیرہ کہ یہ سب بیج میں داخل ہوتے ہیں۔
Upon this, the author of Al-Hidayah said: The meaning of Imam Quduri is that when fruits are mentioned in the sale, because without mention fruits are not included in the sale. Then the author of Al-Hidayah said: The matter that was mentioned is based on istihsan; otherwise, the requirement of qiyas is that the pre-emptor should not get the fruits because fruits are not subordinate to the tree and land. And the evidence for not being subordinate is that fruits are not included in the sale without mention, so its example becomes like goods placed in a house, just as if someone sold a house and there is a refrigerator and gas stove etc. placed in it, these will not be included in the sale. The evidence for istihsan is that fruits are subordinate to the land in terms of connection, just as a building is subordinate to the house, and similarly those items that are fixed in the house, such as doors, doorframes, cabinets, railings, etc., all of these are included in the sale.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the rule of pre-emption regarding fruits. The commentator explains that Imam Quduri's statement means fruits are included in the sale only if mentioned. Then he clarifies the basis of the ruling in Al-Hidayah: the pre-emptor (shafi') takes fruits attached to the land by istihsan (juristic preference), not by qiyas (analogy). According to qiyas, fruits are not subordinate (tabi') to the land because they are not included in the sale without mention, similar to movable goods like a refrigerator or stove. However, istihsan considers that due to physical attachment (ittisal), fruits are like fixed fixtures (doors, cabinets) which are included in the sale. Thus, the pre-emptor takes them because they are attached at the time of taking possession.

Key Terms
استحسان (istihsan): A method of juristic preference where a ruling is adopted because it is more equitable, even if it contradicts a clear analogy.
قیاس (qiyas): Analogical reasoning, extending a ruling from an original case to a new case based on a shared effective cause ('illa).
تبع (taba'): Subordinate or appendage; something that follows the ruling of the principal due to connection or dependence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi school give the pre-emptor (shafi') the right to take attached fruits from the land?
A) Because fruits are mentioned in the sale contract.
B) Because fruits are movable property and thus included.
C) Because fruits are considered subordinate (tabi') to the land due to their physical attachment.
D) Because the buyer has no right to fruits.

Key Takeaway
Attached fruits are taken by the pre-emptor because physical attachment (ittisal) makes them subordinate (tabi') to the land, applying istihsan over qiyas.

Three Scenarios of Fruit in Pre-emption and Their Effect on Price Reduction
Enumeration of three scenarios for fruit and ruling on price reduction when fruit is destroyed

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three scenarios of fruit in shuf'ah and apply the ruling on price reduction when fruit is destroyed, distinguishing between buyer's act and natural calamity.

Core Idea
The right to take attached fruits in pre-emption depends on timing: present at contract gives full price reduction; after possession gives none.

This passage presents a crucial distinction in Hanafi law of pre-emption (shuf'ah) regarding fruits that are attached to the property being sold. The ruling on whether the pre-emptor's price is reduced when those fruits are destroyed depends on when the fruits appeared and what caused their destruction. This lesson will walk through the three scenarios and the underlying reasoning based on istihsan.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three time-points for fruits
List when fruits can exist relative to the contract and possession: (1) at time of contract, (2) after contract but before possession, (3) after possession.
These correspond to the three cases in the passage.
2
Determine the cause of destruction
For each scenario, identify whether the fruits were destroyed by the buyer's act or by a natural calamity (act of God).
The cause matters only in scenario 2.
3
Apply the ruling for each scenario
State the price reduction rule: scenario 1 always reduces; scenario 2 reduces only if buyer caused; scenario 3 never reduces.
Memorize the three outcomes.
4
Understand the istihsan vs qiyas reasoning
Recognize that qiyas would deny fruits to the pre-emptor as movables, but istihsan includes them because they are subordinate (tabi') to the immovable property.
This shows how Hanafi law uses istihsan to achieve equitable outcomes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تنبیہ - علامہ مینی بحوالہ شرح الکافی فرماتے ہیں کہ یہاں تین صورتیں ہیں۔
ا۔ پھل بوقت عقد موجود ہو۔
۲۔ عقید کے بعد قبضہ سے پہلے آئے ہوں۔
۳۔ قبضہ کے بعد آئے ہوں۔
پہلی صورت میں ثمن پھل کے بقد ر ساقط ہو گا خواہ پھل مشتری کے فعل سے ختم ہوئے ہوں یا آسمانی آفت کی وجہ سے اور دوسری صورت میں فعل مشتری کا اگر دخل ہو تو ثمن ساقط اور آفت سماوی کا دخل ہو تو ساقط نہیں۔ اور تیسری صورت میں مطلقا ثمن ساقط نہ ہوگا۔ تنبیه - پھل کو استحسانا لینے کا مسئلہ یہاں بیان کیا گیا ہے اور اس میں یہ بھی بتایا گیا ہے کہ جب اس کی عقد بیع میں شرط ہوئی ہو بظاہر یہ اشکال ہوتا ہے کہ جب استحسانا پھل ملے ہیں تو بغیر شرط کے بھی ملنے چاہئیں۔ سوال مجمع الانہر ص ۴۶۱ ج ۲ سے بھی یہی بات سمجھ میں آتی ہے کہ استحسان کی صورت میں بھی یہ ضروری ہے کہ بیچ میں اس کا ذکر آیا ہو، تو پھر استحسان وقیاس کے ذکر سے کیا فائدہ؟
جواب چونکہ پھل اشیاء منقولہ میں سے ہے جس میں سے شفعہ نہیں چلتا اسلئے قیاس کا تقاضہ یہ تھا کہ شفیع کو نہ ملیں ۔ تو اس کو بتایا کہ یہاں قیاس کو چھوڑ کر استحسان پر عمل کیا گیا ہے اور اعتراض مذکور کا یہ جواب دیا گیا ہے کہ پھل اگر چہ اشیاء منقولہ میں سے ہے لیکن یہاں شی ، غیر منقول کے تابع ہے جس میں شفعہ چلتا ہے اسلئے تابع کو متبوع کے حکم میں لیتے ہوئے بشرط اتصال غیر منقول کے حکم میں لے کر اس میں شفعہ جاری کر دیا گیا ہے۔
Caution - Allama Miani, referencing Sharh al-Kafi, says there are three cases here: 1. Fruits exist at the time of contract. 2. Fruits appear after contract but before possession. 3. Fruits appear after possession. In the first case, the price is reduced by the value of the fruits, whether the fruits are destroyed by the buyer's action or by a sky calamity. In the second case, if the buyer's act intervenes, the price is reduced; if a sky calamity intervenes, it is not reduced. In the third case, the price is absolutely not reduced. Caution - The issue of taking fruits by istihsan is stated here, and it also explains that when it is conditional in the sale contract, apparently there is an objection: since by istihsan fruits are given, they should be given even without condition. A question from Majma' al-Anhar (vol 2, p. 461) also gives the same understanding: even in the case of istihsan, it is necessary that it be mentioned in the sale; so what is the benefit of mentioning istihsan and qiyas? Answer: Since fruits are among movable items in which shuf'ah does not apply, the requirement of qiyas was that the pre-emptor should not get them. So it was explained that here qiyas is abandoned and istihsan is acted upon. And the answer to the mentioned objection is that although fruits are movable, here they are subordinate to immovable property in which shuf'ah applies. Therefore, taking the subordinate according to the ruling of the principal, with the condition of attachment, they are taken in the ruling of immovable and shuf'ah is applied to them.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage is a warning (tanbih) explaining three scenarios for fruits in pre-emption: (1) fruit present at contract, (2) fruit appears after contract but before possession, (3) fruit appears after possession. The ruling on price reduction when fruit is destroyed varies: in scenario 1, the price is reduced by the fruit's value regardless of cause (buyer's act or act of God). In scenario 2, reduction occurs only if the buyer destroys the fruit; if a natural calamity, no reduction. In scenario 3, no reduction at all. A second warning addresses an objection: if fruits are taken by istihsan (not qiyas), why must they be mentioned in the sale? The response is that even though fruits are movable, they become subordinate (tabi') to the immovable property due to physical attachment, so like fixtures, they are included in the pre-emption. Thus, istihsan overrides qiyas.

Key Terms
ثمن (thaman): The price paid by the buyer in the sale; here it refers to the amount the pre-emptor must pay to take the property.
آفت سماوی (aafat samawiyyah): A natural calamity (act of God) such as storm, fire, etc., that destroys fruits.
استحسان (istihsan): Equitable preference; a method of juristic reasoning that departs from strict qiyas for a more equitable outcome.
قیاس (qiyas): Analogical reasoning; deriving a ruling for a new case from an existing ruling due to shared illa.
شفعہ (shuf'ah): Pre-emption; the right of a co-owner or neighbor to purchase property sold to an outsider.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In which of the three scenarios is the price reduced even if the fruit is destroyed by a natural calamity?
A) Fruits exist at time of contract.
B) Fruits appear after contract but before possession.
C) Fruits appear after possession.
D) Price is never reduced for natural calamities.

Key Takeaway
The pre-emptor's right to attached fruits and price reduction depends on when the fruits existed and the cause of destruction, with natural calamities only leading to reduction if fruits were present at the time of contract.
