There is an objection which we clarified in the Book of Sales, so we will not repeat it. And when the buyer takes this house [via shuf'a], this becomes permission for the sale from his side. Contrary to the case when the buyer purchased it and had not seen it, in that respect his option is not voided. Because of taking this house in shuf'a which was sold adjacent to it. Because the option of inspection is not voided by explicit annulment, so how can it be voided by implication of annulment? Then when the shafi' of the first house appears, he has the right to take the first house, not the second, because the ownership of the shafi' was non-existent when the second was sold. Explanation: In the first issue, there was mention of the seller's option, and here mention of the buyer's option. And it is already known that in the case of the buyer's option, the sold item exits the seller's ownership. It is learned that in this case shuf'a will be given. Because the basis of shuf'a is on the removal of the seller's ownership. In any case, when the buyer took an option for himself, he has the choice within three days either to cancel the sale or to make it binding and effective. But if within three days the shafi' takes this house via shuf'a, then the sale becomes automatically binding because the buyer is no longer able to cancel it. Then a question arises: the option that is for the buyer, will it also be for the shafi'? The answer is no, because khiyar al-shart is acquired by stipulation, and this contract occurred between the seller and the buyer, not between the seller and the shafi'.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah discusses the interaction between khiyar al-shart (option of condition) owned by the buyer and the right of pre-emption (shuf'a). The commentary begins by referencing an objection that was resolved in the Book of Sales, then states the ruling: if a buyer who has an option takes the adjacent property via shuf'a, this act constitutes implicit permission for the sale, making it binding and waiving his own option. However, an exception is made for khiyar al-ru'ya (option of inspection): if the buyer had not seen the property, his option of inspection is not voided by merely taking it via shuf'a, because that option is only extinguished by explicit renunciation, not by implication. The text then presents a second scenario: if the original shafi' (the neighbor of the first house) later appears after the buyer has taken the second house via shuf'a, the shafi' may only claim the first house, not the second, because at the time of the second sale the shafi' did not own the first house (ownership was with the buyer). The commentary clarifies that in this issue, the buyer's option differs from the seller's option, and reiterates that the buyer's option causes the sold item to leave the seller's ownership, thus shuf'a applies. Finally, a question is answered: the shafi' does not inherit the buyer's option because khiyar al-shart is a contractual right between the original parties only.

Key Terms
خیار شرط (khiyar al-shart): An option stipulated in the contract for one or both parties to annul or confirm the sale within a specified period.
خیار رؤیت (khiyar al-ru'ya): The option of the buyer to annul the sale if he purchases an unseen item and finds it unsatisfactory upon inspection.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a buyer with a three-day khiyar al-shart takes the adjacent property via shuf'a within the option period, what happens to the buyer's option?
A) The buyer retains the option for the remaining days.
B) The option is voided and the sale becomes binding.
C) The option transfers to the shafi'.
D) The sale is annulled.

Key Takeaway
A buyer's khiyar al-shart is waived by taking shuf'a, but khiyar al-ru'ya remains; the shafi' does not acquire the option.

Shuf'a for a Neighbor with an Option
Determines whether a neighbor who has an option in a property being sold has a right of pre-emption.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding shuf'a for a buyer with an option and state the operative ruling.

Core Idea
Taking shuf'a by a buyer with an option establishes ownership by implicitly dropping the option.

This passage addresses a nuanced question: can a neighbor who has an option in a property being sold claim the right of pre-emption (shuf'a)? The Hanafi school agrees that both seller and buyer with an option may take shuf'a, but the reasoning differs between Imam Abu Hanifa and his two students. Understanding this dispute clarifies how ownership during khiyar is treated in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the issue
Determine the legal question: Does a buyer with an option (khiyar) have the right of shuf'a?
2
State the ruling
The ruling is that both seller and buyer with an option are entitled to shuf'a.
The ruling is unanimous within the Hanafi school.
3
Present the Sahibayn's position
According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, the buyer is considered owner during the option period, so shuf'a is straightforward.
For them, ownership transfers immediately.
4
Present Abu Hanifa's objection
Imam Abu Hanifa argues that the buyer is not yet owner (property left seller but not entered buyer), yet shuf'a is given – this seems inconsistent.
This is the core dispute.
5
Provide the resolution
The commentary explains that by actually claiming shuf'a, the buyer implicitly drops his option and confirms the sale, thereby establishing his ownership.
Thus the ruling is consistent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وان بيعة.... الخ ۔ یہاں سے دوسرا مسئلہ بیان کرتے ہیں کہ اگر گھر کے برابر میں کوئی گھر بک رہا ہے جس کے اندر بائع یا مشتری کو خیار ہے تو ان کیلئے حق شفعہ ہوگا یا نہیں تو فرمایا دونوں میں سے جو نسے کیلئے بھی خیار ہوگا اس کو شفعہ ملے گا۔ رہی یہ بات کہ بائع کو کیوں ملے گا تو اس کا جواب ظاہر ہے کہ جب خیار بائع کیلئے ہوگا تو مکان اسکی ملکیت ہوتا ہے اس کو شفعہ ملے گا اور اگر خیار مشتری کو ہو تو اس کو بھی شفعہ ملے گا۔ اس پر صاحب ہدایہ فرماتے ہیں۔ وفيه اشكال اوضحناه في كتاب البيوع فلا نعيده اشکال یہ ہے کہ یہ بات صاحبین کے مطابق تو صحیح ہے کیونکہ ان کے نزدیک مکان مالک مشتری ہے۔ مگر امام ابوحنیفہ کے نزدیک اگر چہ گھر بائع کی ملکیت سے نکل گیا مگر مشتری کی ملکیت میں تو داخل نہیں ہوا اور شفعہ ملک کی بنیاد پر ملتا ہے تو پھر شفیع کو شفعہ کیوں ملا ؟ یہ ہے اشکال ، مگر جواب یہ ہے کہ جب اس نے شفعہ لینے پر اقدام کیا تو یہ اقدام اس بات کی دلیل ہے کہ اس نے خیار کو ساقط کر کے بیع نافذ ولازم کر دی اور اب اس کی ملکیت ظاہر ہے۔ اور یہ اشکال و جواب مصنف نے ہدایہ میں ذکر نہیں کیا شاید کفایۃ المنتہی میں بیان کیا ہو۔
And if a house is sold... etc. From here we explain the second issue: if a house adjacent to a house is being sold and the seller or buyer has an option, will they have the right of shuf'a? So it is said: whoever has the option, whether seller or buyer, will get shuf'a. As for why the seller gets it, the answer is obvious: when the option is for the seller, the house is his property, so he gets shuf'a. And if the option is for the buyer, he also gets shuf'a. Upon this, the author of al-Hidayah says: 'There is an objection in this, which we clarified in the Book of Sales, so we do not repeat it.' The objection is that this is correct according to the Sahibayn because according to them the house is owned by the buyer. But according to Imam Abu Hanifa, although the house left the seller's ownership, it did not enter the buyer's ownership, and shuf'a is based on ownership. So why did the shafi' get shuf'a? This is the objection. But the answer is that when he undertook to take shuf'a, this action is evidence that he dropped the option and made the sale binding and effective, and now his ownership is apparent. And the author did not mention this objection and answer in al-Hidayah; perhaps it was mentioned in Kifayat al-Muntaha.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage introduces the second issue: whether a neighbor who has an option (khiyar) in a property being sold has a right of pre-emption. The ruling is that both the seller and the buyer, if they hold an option, are entitled to shuf'a. The reasoning for the seller is straightforward: the house remains his property during the option period. For the buyer, it is also given because the buyer's option transfers effective ownership for the purpose of shuf'a. However, an intra-madhhab dispute arises: according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn), the buyer becomes owner during the option period, so shuf'a is consistent. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the buyer does not become full owner, so how can he claim shuf'a which requires ownership? The commentary provides the answer: by actually exercising shuf'a, the buyer implicitly confirms the sale (drops his option), thereby establishing his ownership. This resolution is not in the Hidayah itself but is borrowed from Kifayat al-Muntaha.

Key Terms
صاحبین (al-Sahibayn): Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, the two main students of Abu Hanifa.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, why does a buyer with an option still get shuf'a?
A) Because the buyer is considered owner from the contract date.
B) Because taking shuf'a serves as evidence of dropping the option and confirming ownership.
C) Because the seller's option has ended.
D) Because the property is jointly owned.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a buyer with an option has the right of shuf'a because taking it implicitly confirms the sale and establishes ownership.

Shuf'ah After Rescission Ends: Timing and Possession
Expansion of shuf'ah rulings in defective sales

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on shuf'ah when the right of rescission falls away and apply the timing condition (before/after judicial judgment) to determine the validity of shuf'ah.

Core Idea
Shuf'ah is established when the impediment is removed, but its validity depends on ownership at the time of judgment.

This passage from Ashraf al-Hidayah addresses a nuanced issue in shuf'ah (pre-emption) involving defective sales. When a sale is fasid, the buyer has a right to rescind, which blocks shuf'ah. But if that right falls away (e.g., the buyer sells the property to a third party), the impediment is removed and shuf'ah becomes available. The passage then presents multiple scenarios focusing on the timing of delivery or repossession of the property relative to the judge's verdict. Understanding these scenarios is crucial for applying shuf'ah correctly in practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general principle
Recognize that when the right of rescission falls away, the impediment to shuf'ah is removed, and shuf'ah becomes wajib.
This is the opening statement in the passage.
2
Distinguish possession scenarios
Determine who is the pre-emptor based on who owns the adjacent property at the time of the shuf'ah claim: if the seller still possesses the defective property, he is the pre-emptor; if he has delivered it to the buyer, the buyer is.
The passage gives two base cases: seller's possession vs. delivery to buyer.
3
Apply the timing rule to the seller's delivery
If the seller delivers the adjacent property to the buyer before the judgment of shuf'ah in his favor, his shuf'ah becomes void. If delivery occurs after judgment, shuf'ah is upheld.
Analogous to the seller selling his property before judgment.
4
Apply the timing rule to repossession by the seller
If the seller takes back the defective property from the buyer due to the defect before the buyer's shuf'ah judgment, the buyer's right to shuf'ah becomes void. If repossession occurs after judgment, the buyer keeps the shuf'ah property.
The key is whether ownership of the adjacent property is severed before judgment.
5
Synthesize the underlying principle
Understand that continuation of ownership in the property by which one claims shuf'ah is not a condition after the judgment; only ownership at the time of claim and judgment matters.
This principle is stated explicitly in the passage: 'continuation after judgment is not a condition.'

باقی رہنا بالاتفاق شرط نہیں ہے۔ وان استردها.. الخ مبیع بیع فاسد میں مشتری کے قبضہ میں جا چکا اور اس کے برابر میں جو مکان فروخت ہوا۔ مشتری نے اس میں شفعہ کا دعوی کیا اور اسی حالت میں بیع کے اندر فساد کی وجہ سے بحکم شرعی بائع نے مبیع کو مشتری سے واپس لے لیا تو اب مشتری کو شفعہ ملے گا یا نہیں؟ تو جواب دیا کہ اگر بائع نے مشتری کے حق میں شفعہ کا حکم ہونے سے پہلے واپس لے لیا تو مشتری کو شفعہ نہیں ملے گا اور اگر حکم شفعہ ہو جانے کے بعد واپس لیا ہو تو واپسی درست ہے اور جو مکان مشتری شفعہ میں لے چکا ہے وہ اس کا ہے۔ دلیل وہی ہے جو بائع کے بیان میں گزر چکی ہے۔
He said: When the right of rescission falls, shuf'ah becomes obligatory due to the removal of the impediment. And if a house is sold adjacent to it (the pre-emptor's property) while that house is still in the seller's possession, then he has shuf'ah because his ownership remains. And if he hands it over to the buyer, then the buyer is the pre-emptor because ownership is his. Then if the seller hands over before judgment of shuf'ah in his favor, his shuf'ah is void, just as if he sold it, unlike if he hands over after, because the continuation of his ownership in the house by which he claims shuf'ah after the judgment of shuf'ah is not a condition, so the house taken by shuf'ah remains his property. And if the seller takes it back from the buyer before judgment of shuf'ah in his favor, it is void because his ownership of the house by which he claims shuf'ah is cut off before the judgment of shuf'ah. And if he takes it back after the judgment, the second house remains his property because of what we have explained. Translation: The author said: So if the right of rescission falls, shuf'ah is established due to removal of impediment. And if a house is sold adjacent to it while this house is still in the seller's possession, then he has shuf'ah due to continuation of his ownership. And if the seller hands it over to the buyer, the buyer is his pre-emptor because ownership is his. Then if the seller hands over the house before judgment of shuf'ah for himself, his shuf'ah becomes void, just as when he sold it. Unlike the case when he hands over after, because the continuation of the pre-emptor's ownership in the house by which he claims shuf'ah after the judgment is not a condition; the house taken by shuf'ah remains his property. And if the seller takes back the house from the buyer before judgment of shuf'ah for the buyer, then shuf'ah becomes void due to the buyer's disconnection from the house by which he claims shuf'ah before the judgment. And if the seller takes it back after the judgment, the second house remains the buyer's property due to the evidence we have stated. Explanation: All this previous discussion was when there is possibility of rescission in a defective sale. But if the possibility of rescission has ended, for example if the buyer sold that house to someone else, then the pre-emptor gets shuf'ah because the impediment has been removed. Now a second issue: In a defective sale, the sold item is still in the seller's possession. If coincidentally a house adjacent to it is sold, the seller gets shuf'ah because the house is his property. Yes, if the seller had handed it over to the buyer, then shuf'ah goes to the buyer because now the buyer is its owner. Second scenario: The sold item was in the seller's possession, a house adjacent to it was sold, and the seller claimed shuf'ah. But before the judge decided shuf'ah for the seller, the seller handed over the sold item to the buyer. Then the seller does not get shuf'ah. Just as if he sold it — this case is exactly like when someone claimed shuf'ah but before the judge decided for him, he sold his own house by which he was claiming shuf'ah, then his shuf'ah becomes void. Similarly here shuf'ah is void. Unlike if the seller claimed shuf'ah and the judge decided for him, and after that the seller handed over the sold item to the buyer, then the house which the seller has taken by shuf'ah is his; shuf'ah is not void. Because it is an established principle that after the judge's decision, continuation of ownership in the house by which shuf'ah was taken is not required by consensus. And if he takes it back... The sold item in a defective sale had come into the buyer's possession, and a house adjacent to it was sold. The buyer claimed shuf'ah. In this state, due to the defect in the sale, the seller legally took back the sold item from the buyer. Then does the buyer get shuf'ah or not? The answer: If the seller took it back before the judgment of shuf'ah for the buyer, then the buyer does not get shuf'ah. If he took it back after the judgment of shuf'ah, the taking back is valid, and the house that the buyer took by shuf'ah is his. The evidence is the same that passed in the seller's explanation.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement with commentary from Ashraf al-Hidayah, explaining the impact of the right of rescission (khiyar al-faskh) on shuf'ah (pre-emption). The author presents multiple scenarios based on the status of possession and timing of judgment.

First, the general principle: when the right of rescission falls away (e.g., because the buyer sold the property to a third party), the impediment to shuf'ah is removed, and shuf'ah becomes obligatory.

Then the passage discusses a defective sale (bay' fasid) where the sold item remains in the seller's possession. If a neighboring house is sold, the seller has shuf'ah because he still owns the adjacent property. If the seller has already delivered the sold item to the buyer, the buyer becomes the pre-emptor since he now owns the property.

Next, the timing of delivery relative to the court's judgment on shuf'ah is crucial. If the seller (or buyer, as the case may be) delivers the adjacent property before the judge rules in his favor, his right to shuf'ah becomes void—analogous to selling his own property before judgment. But if delivery occurs after the judgment, the shuf'ah is upheld because continuation of ownership in the property by which one claims shuf'ah is not a condition after the judge's decision.

Finally, the passage addresses what happens if the seller takes back the sold item from the buyer due to the defect in the original sale. If this repossession occurs before the shuf'ah judgment, the buyer's right to shuf'ah is void because he no longer owns the adjacent property. If after the judgment, the shuf'ah remains valid.

All rulings follow the Hanafi school and are based on the principle that shuf'ah is established by ownership at the time of claim and judgment, but not necessarily thereafter.

Key Terms
الشفعة (al-shuf'ah): Pre-emption: the right of a co-owner or neighbor to purchase a property that has been sold to a third party, under certain conditions.
حق الفسخ (haqq al-faskh): The right to rescind a contract, especially in a defective sale where the buyer has the option to cancel.
الحكم بالشفعة (al-hukm bi al-shuf'ah): The court's judgment awarding shuf'ah to the pre-emptor.
الفسخ (al-faskh): Rescission: cancellation of a contract, often due to a defect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a seller takes back the sold item from the buyer before the judge awards shuf'ah to the buyer, what happens to the buyer's right to shuf'ah?
A) The buyer's shuf'ah becomes void.
B) The buyer's shuf'ah remains valid.
C) The buyer gets a new right to shuf'ah.
D) The seller gets the shuf'ah instead.

Key Takeaway
Shuf'ah is determined by ownership at the time of judgment; changes before judgment void the right, but changes after do not affect it.

No Shuf'ah for Neighbour After Partition
Ruling statement with ta'lil (legal reasoning) explaining why partition does not trigger shuf'ah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on shuf'ah in partition and explain the legal reasoning that distinguishes partition from sale.

Core Idea
Partition is segregation, not exchange—no sale, no shuf'ah.

In the Hanafi school, the right of pre-emption (shuf'ah) attaches only to absolute exchanges, such as sales. But what happens when co-owners simply divide their jointly owned land? Does a neighbour get shuf'ah? This passage clarifies that partition is fundamentally different from sale: it is a segregation of existing shares, not a transfer of ownership. Consequently, shuf'ah does not arise.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling
Read the opening statement: when partners divide real estate, there is no shuf'ah for their neighbour. This is the explicit Hanafi position.
2
Locate the legal reason (ta'lil)
The author gives the reason: because partition has the meaning of segregation (ifraz). Understand that partition is not an exchange of ownership but a separation of pre-existing shares.
The Arabic uses 'القسمة فيها معنى الافراز'.
3
Distinguish partition from sale
Notice the contrasting features: in partition, compulsion (jabr) is allowed if a co-owner refuses, while in sale, mutual consent is required. Shuf'ah is only legislated for absolute exchange (mubadalah mutlaqah), not for segregation.
This distinction is crucial for the reasoning.
4
Apply the principle
Given a scenario of partition, determine if any condition for shuf'ah is present. Since there is no sale, shuf'ah is inapplicable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال واذا اقتسم الشركاء العقار فلا شفعة لجارهم بالقسمة لان القسمة فيها معنى الافراز ولهذا يجرى فيه الجبر والشفعة ما شرعت الا فى المبادلة المطلقة. ترجمہ اور جبکہ شرکاء نے زمین کا بٹوارہ کیا تو بنوارہ کی وجہ سے ان کے پڑوسی کیلئے شفعہ نہیں ہے اسلئے کہ قسمت اس میں افراز کے معنی ہیں (الگ الگ کرنا) اور اسی وجہ سے اس میں جبر جاری ہوتا ہے اور شفعہ مشروع نہیں ہوا مگر مبادلہ مطلقہ میں۔ تشریح شرکاء نے اپنی زمین کا بٹوارہ کیا تو ظاہر ہے کہ یہ بیچ نہیں ہے اور مبادلہ کالی نہیں ہے بلکہ اپنے حصوں کو بانٹنا اور جدا جدا کرنا ہے جس کو افراز کہا گیا ہے۔ لہذا اگر کوئی پڑوسی بوقت بٹوارہ شفعہ کا دعوی کرے تو اس کو شفعہ نہیں ملے گا۔ کیونکہ شرط شفعہ (بیع) نہیں پائی گئی اور قسمت میں زمین و آسمان کا فرق ہے۔ بیع کے اندر جبر جائز نہیں بلکہ تراضی شرط ہے اور قسمت کے اندر جبر جائز ہے یعنی اگر شریکوں میں سے کوئی قسمت کا مطالبہ کرے اور دوسرا شریک انکار کرے تو منکر پر جبر کیا جائے گا اور اس کو ہوارہ پر مجبور کیا جائے گا۔
He said: When the partners divide real estate, there is no shuf'ah for their neighbour because of the division, because division has the meaning of segregation, and therefore compulsion is applicable in it, and shuf'ah has only been legislated in absolute exchange. Translation: And when the partners divided the land, then their neighbour does not have shuf'ah due to the division, because division contains the meaning of segregation (separating), and for this reason compulsion is applicable in it, and shuf'ah has not been legislated except in absolute exchange. Explanation: The partners divided their land. It is clear that this is not a sale and is not an exchange, but rather it is dividing and separating their shares, which is called segregation. Therefore, if a neighbour claims shuf'ah at the time of division, he will not get shuf'ah, because the condition of shuf'ah (sale) is not found, and there is a difference like heaven and earth between division and sale. In sale, compulsion is not permissible; rather, mutual consent is a condition. In division, compulsion is permissible, meaning if one of the partners demands division and the other refuses, then the refuser can be compelled to divide.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This is a clear ruling statement from the Hanafi school, supported by a legal reasoning (ta'lil). The author, quoting the matn, states that when co-owners partition their real estate, no right of shuf'ah (pre-emption) arises for a neighbour. The reason given is that partition is essentially segregation (ifraz) of existing shares, not an exchange or sale. Shuf'ah is only legislated for absolute exchanges (mubadalah mutlaqah), i.e., sales. The Urdu commentary elaborates that partition is not a sale; it is simply separating what each already owns. Consequently, the condition for shuf'ah—a sale—is missing. Furthermore, partition differs from sale in that compulsion (jabr) is permitted in partition: if one co-owner wants to divide and the other refuses, the latter can be compelled by the court. In a sale, compulsion is not allowed; mutual consent is required. Thus, shuf'ah does not apply to partitions.

Key Terms
القسمة (al-qismah): Partition or division of jointly owned property among co-owners.
الافراز (al-ifraz): Segregation; separating the shares of co-owners without any exchange of ownership.
المبادلة المطلقة (al-mubadalah al-mutlaqah): Absolute exchange; a transaction where one property is exchanged for another or for price, without any other considerations.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is shuf'ah not applicable in the partition of jointly owned land according to the Hanafi school?
A) Because shuf'ah is only for movable property.
B) Because partition is not a sale but a segregation of shares.
C) Because the neighbour must be a co-owner.
D) Because the judge must approve the partition first.

Key Takeaway
Partition is a segregation of existing shares, not an exchange; therefore, the right of pre-emption (shuf'ah) does not arise.

Shuf'ah Does Not Revive After Waiver and Buyer's Return
Ruling that if pre-emptor waives shuf'ah and then the buyer returns the property, the right does not revive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a pre-emptor waives shuf'ah and then the buyer returns the property, and explain the legal reasoning based on the nature of rescission (faskh).

Core Idea
Once a pre-emptor waives shuf'ah, the right is lost permanently—even if the sale is later rescinded—because shuf'ah arises only from a new contract.

This lesson examines a specific scenario in shuf'ah (pre-emption). The pre-emptor has explicitly waived his right. Later, the buyer exercises an option (e.g., due to a defect) and returns the property. Does the pre-emptor get a second chance? The Hanafi school gives a definitive answer based on the nature of contract creation and rescission.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
Determine the parties: buyer purchases, pre-emptor waives, buyer returns via khiyar. The ruling states no shuf'ah for the pre-emptor.
The waiver is a deliberate relinquishment of the right.
2
Trace the reasoning: nature of rescission
The buyer's return is a full rescission (faskh min kulli wajh), meaning the sale is retroactively cancelled. The property returns to the seller's original ownership.
This contrasts with partial rescission where some effects remain.
3
Apply the principle: shuf'ah and contract creation
Shuf'ah is a right that arises from the creation of a new sale contract (insha' al-'aqd). Since the rescission cancels the sale, no new contract exists for shuf'ah to attach to.
The waiver remains effective because the condition for shuf'ah (a valid sale) is no longer present.
4
Note the indifference to possession
The ruling applies whether or not the buyer had taken possession before returning the property. Possession does not affect the analysis.
This eliminates a potential exception.
5
Recognize cross-madhhab ikhtilaf
Some schools may hold that shuf'ah revives if the sale is annulled, but the Hanafi school firmly denies it. The ikhtilaf stems from differing views on the effect of rescission.
Knowing this helps in understanding the Hanafi position in comparative fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال واذا اشترى دارا فسلم الشفيع الشفعة ثم ردها المشترى بخيار رؤية او شرط او بعيب بقضاء قاض فلا شفعة للشفيع لانه فسخ من كل وجه فعاد الى قديم ملكه والشفعة انشاء العقد ولا فرق في هذا بين القبض وعدمه
ترجمہ امام قدوری نے فرمایا اور جب مشتری نے گھر خریدا پس شفیع نے شفعہ سے دست برداری دے دی ۔ پھر مشتری نے اس گھر کو خیا روایت یا خیار شرط یا عیب کی وجہ سے قاضی کے فیصلہ کے ساتھ واپس کیا تو شفیع کیلئے شفعہ نہیں ہے اسلئے کہ یہ ہر اعتبار سے فتح ہے تو میرے نزدیک اس میں قبضہ اور عدم قبضہ میں کوئی فرق نہیں ہے۔
He said: When someone buys a house, and the pre-emptor waives his right of shuf'ah, and then the buyer returns it due to an option of inspection, or an option of condition, or a defect, by judgment of a judge, then there is no shuf'ah for the pre-emptor, because it is a rescission from all aspects, so it returns to the original ownership, and shuf'ah is based on the creation of the contract. There is no difference in this between taking possession and not taking possession. Translation: Imam Quduri said: When the buyer bought a house, and the pre-emptor waived his right of shuf'ah, then the buyer returned this house due to an option of inspection, or an option of condition, or a defect, with the judgment of a judge, then there is no shuf'ah for the pre-emptor. Because this is a rescission from all aspects, so... In my opinion, there is no difference in this between possession and non-possession.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage presents a decisive Hanafi ruling from Al-Quduri's matn, as explained in Ashraf al-Hidayah. The scenario: A buyer purchases a house, and the pre-emptor (shafi') expressly waives his right of shuf'ah. Later, the buyer returns the house to the seller due to one of three options: khiyar al-ru'yah (option of inspection after purchase), khiyar al-shart (option stipulated in the contract), or khiyar al-'ayb (option due to defect). The return is effected by a judge's ruling. The question is whether the pre-emptor's right to shuf'ah revives. The ruling: The pre-emptor does not regain the right. The reasoning: The buyer's return of the property is a full rescission (faskh min kulli wajh), which annuls the sale from the beginning. The property returns to the original ownership of the seller, as if the sale never occurred. Since shuf'ah is a right that arises from the creation of a new contract (insha' al-'aqd), and here there is no new contract, the pre-emptor's waiver remains effective. It does not matter whether the buyer had taken possession of the house or not before returning it; the ruling is the same. The commentary notes that the rescission here is 'from all aspects' (min kulli wajh), meaning it retroactively cancels the sale entirely, unlike some other forms of rescission that might be considered partial.

Key Terms
فسخ (faskh): Rescission; the annulment of a contract, here referring to the buyer returning the property due to an option.
خیار رویت (khiyar al-ru'yah): Option of inspection; the buyer's right to cancel the contract after seeing the item if it was not seen before purchase.
خیار شرط (khiyar al-shart): Option by stipulation; the right for either party to cancel within a specified period as per the contract.
خیار عیب (khiyar al-'ayb): Option due to defect; the buyer's right to return the item if a defect is found.
انشاء العقد (insha' al-'aqd): Creation of the contract; the formation of a new contractual relationship.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the scenario described in the passage, what is the reason the pre-emptor does not regain shuf'ah after the buyer returns the property?
A) Because the pre-emptor's waiver was not witnessed by two just witnesses.
B) Because the rescission is full and shuf'ah requires a new contract.
C) Because the buyer had not taken possession of the property.
D) Because the judge's decision is final and cannot be appealed.

Key Takeaway
A pre-emptor who waives shuf'ah permanently loses the right; it does not revive even if the sale is later rescinded through a buyer's option.

Void Compensation for Dropping a Personal Right
Explains the analogy of shuf'a with cases of void compensation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that khiyar (a personal right) cannot be traded and apply the principle to void compensation scenarios.

Core Idea
A personal right like khiyar cannot be traded; dropping it for money renders the compensation void.

This passage from Hidayah illustrates a key Hanafi principle: certain rights established by Shariah are not saleable commodities. When a person is paid to drop such a right, the payment is not due. The author draws two analogies from marriage law to reinforce the same rule applied to shuf'a (pre-emption).

Guided Path
1
Identify the core principle
Recognize that in each case, a right (khiyar, option of annulment, or shuf'a) is dropped, but the promised compensation is void because the right is not a marketable asset.
The key is that the right is purely personal and cannot be sold.
2
Examine the first analogy: husband and wife with khiyar
A husband gives his wife the option of divorce, then offers money to choose him. She does, so her khiyar drops, but the money is not wajib (obligatory).
The wife's right is not a property; the compensation is invalid.
3
Examine the second analogy: impotent husband's wife
The wife of an impotent man has the right to annul the marriage. The husband offers her money to forgo annulment. She agrees, but the compensation is void.
Same principle: annulment right is not saleable.
4
Connect to shuf'a
Just as the wife's khiyar cannot be purchased, the right of pre-emption (shuf'a) cannot be sold. If a pre-emptor accepts money to drop his right, the money is not due.
The right of shuf'a is a Shariah‑established right, not a commodity.
5
Note the proverb
The phrase 'the washerman's cloth: neither the house's nor the ghat's' means the matter is settled between the two parties without a third‑party claim.
This emphasizes that the right is dropped without any compensation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الشفعة
۱۰۴
اشرف الہدایہ شرح اردو ہدایہ جلد - ۱۳
ترجمہ اور اس کی نظیر جبکہ شوہر نے مخیرہ سے کہا کہ ہزار کے بدلہ میں مجھے کو اختیار کر لے یا عمنین نے اپنی بیوی سے کہا کہ ہزار کے بدلہ میں ترک فسخ کو اختیار کرلے پس اس نے اختیار کر لیا تو خیار ساقط ہو گیا اور عوض ثابت نہ ہوگا۔
تشریح صاحب ہدایہ فرماتے ہیں کہ حق شفعہ کی نظیر یہ مسائل ہیں کہ جہاں عوض بھی ثابت نہیں ہوتا اور خیار ساقط ہو جاتا ہے۔ کسی شخص نے اپنی عورت کو خیار دیتے ہوئے کہا اختاری نفسک جس کی تفصیل ہدایہ جلد ثانی میں گذر چکی ہے۔ اب شوہر نے خطرہ محسوس کیا کہ کہیں عورت اپنے نفس کو اختیار نہ کرلے اور طلاق بائن واقع ہو جائے اس خطرہ کے پیش نظر اس نے بیوی سے کہا کہ
ہزار کے بدلہ میں تو مجھے ہی اختیار کر لے ۔ عورت نے کر لیا تو عورت کا خیار ساقط ہو گیا اور ہزار روپے واجب نہ ہوں گے۔ عنین کی بیوی کو اختیار نسخ ملتا ہے جس کی تفصیل ہدایہ جلد ثانی میں گذر چکی ہے مگر شوہر نے اس سے کہا کہ تو نکاح فسخ نہ کر اور میں تجھے ہزار روپے دوں گا تو عورت نے فتح کو ترک کر دیا، اب بھی عورت کا خیار ختم ہو گیا اور عوض بھی باطل ہو گیا۔ یعنی یہ دونوں مسئلے حق شفعہ کی نظیر ہیں جن کی مثال یہ ہے کہ دھوبی کا کتان گھر کا نہ گھاٹ کا ۔
Translation and its analogy: When the husband said to the mukhirah (woman given option), 'Choose me in exchange for a thousand,' or the husband of an impotent man said to his wife, 'Choose to abandon faskh in exchange for a thousand,' and she chose, then the option (khiyar) is dropped and the compensation is not established. Explanation: The author of Hidayah says that the analogy for the right of shuf'a are these cases where compensation is not established and the option is dropped. A man gave his wife the option saying 'Choose yourself' (the details passed in volume two of Hidayah). The husband feared that the wife might choose herself and a bain talaq would occur. So he said to his wife, 'Choose me in exchange for a thousand.' She did, so her option dropped and the thousand rupees are not wajib. The wife of an impotent man has the option of annulment (as detailed in volume two of Hidayah), but the husband said, 'Do not annul the marriage and I will give you a thousand rupees.' The woman left off annulment, so her option terminated and the compensation also became void. These two cases are analogies for the right of shuf'a, and the example is 'the washerman's cloth: neither the house's nor the ghat's.'
Source pages p. 105

Commentary
This passage is a ruling-statement illustrating the principle that when a legal right is dropped in exchange for compensation that is itself invalid (because the right is not a marketable commodity), the right drops but the compensation is not due. The author gives two analogies from family law: first, a husband who has given his wife the option of divorce (khiyar) then offers her money to choose him—she does, but the money is not owed because the option is not saleable. Second, the wife of an impotent man who is given money to forgo her right to annul the marriage—she does, but again the compensation is void. The famous proverb 'the washerman's cloth' is used to indicate that the matter is settled between two parties without a third party's claim. This directly parallels the case of shuf'a where a pre-emptor might accept money to drop his right, but the compensation is void because the right is established by Shariah and cannot be sold.

Key Terms
خیار (khiyar): An option or right to choose, here specifically the wife's option to divorce herself or to annul the marriage.
عوض ('iwad): Compensation or consideration, here the promised money that becomes void when offered in exchange for dropping an option.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the analogy given for shuf'a, when a husband offers his wife money to choose him instead of exercising her option, what is the ruling on the money?
A) The money is wajib (obligatory) on the husband.
B) The money is not wajib (not due).
C) The money is only half wajib.
D) The marriage becomes void.

Key Takeaway
A personal right (khiyar) cannot be sold; dropping it for compensation renders the compensation void, as illustrated by analogies in Hidayah.

When Misinformation About Price or Currency Invalidates Shuf'ah Surrender
Ruling on validity of shuf'ah surrender after misinformation about price

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that distinguishes when a shafi' who surreneders based on misinformation retains or loses the right of shuf'ah.

Core Idea
Surrender is void only if the actual price differs in amount or mithli genus; it stands if the actual price is a commodity or gold dinars of equal value.

A shafi' (pre-emptor) may surrender his right after hearing the sale price, only to discover later that the price as reported was wrong. Does he get his right back? The Hanafi school provides a detailed distinction: if the error is in the amount (less) or in the type of price (e.g., dirhams vs. wheat), the surrender is void. But if the actual price is a commodity of equal value, or gold dinars worth the same dirhams, then surrender stands. This lesson unpacks the ruling and its proof, including the intra-Hanafi disagreement over dirhams versus dinars.

Guided Path
1
Identify the shafi's surrender scenario
Start with the basic facts: the shafi' was informed that the property was sold for a certain price (e.g., 1000 dirhams) and he surrendered. Later he learns the truth is different.
The ruling hinges on what the truth turns out to be.
2
Check if actual price differs in amount
If the actual price is less than reported (e.g., 500 instead of 1000), the surrender is void. The shafi' was induced to surrender by the false notion of a higher price.
This applies regardless of genus – only amount matters.
3
Check if actual price is in a different mithli genus
If the actual price is wheat, barley, or any other makil/mawzun/adadi mutaqarib of equal or greater value, surrender is also void. The genus (mithli) is different, making it harder or easier for the shafi' to pay.
Examples: makil (wheat, barley), mawzun (oil, gold by weight), adadi mutaqarib (eggs, nuts).
4
Distinguish when surrender is valid
If the actual price is a commodity ('ard) whose value equals the reported amount, surrender is valid. The obligation is the value (qimah), which is dirhams/dinars. Similarly, if the actual price is gold dinars of equal value, surrender is valid because dirhams and dinars share thamaniyyah (priceness).
'Ard means non-mithli goods like furniture, livestock, etc. Dinars are not mithli in genus but are treated as same due to thamaniyyah.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Imam Zufar argues that dirhams and dinars are different genera, so surrender should be void. The majority (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) hold the opposite, relying on the concept of thamaniyyah. The mufta bihi is the majority view.
The practical consequence (thamarah) is whether the shafi' retains the right in the dinar case.

Source Text

He (Imam al-Quduri) said: When the shafi' is informed that it was sold for a thousand dirhams, and he surrenders, then he learns that it was sold for less, or for wheat or barley whose value is a thousand or more, then his surrender is void and he has the right of pre-emption, because he surrendered in the first case due to deeming the price great, and in the second case due to the difficulty of the genus he was informed of and the ease of what it was sold for, because the genus is different. And likewise, every measurable (makil) or weighable (mawzun) or numerable that is similar (adadi mutaqarib). Translation: Imam Quduri said: When the shafi' is informed that the house was sold for a thousand dirhams, so he surrenders, then he learns that it was sold for less, or for wheat or barley whose value is a thousand or more, then his surrender is void and he has the right of pre-emption, because he surrendered in the first case due to deeming the price great, and in the second case due to the difficulty of the genus he was informed of and the ease of that with which it was sold, because the genus is different. And likewise every measurable, weighable, or similar numerable. Explanation: Someone told the shafi' that the house was sold for a thousand dirhams, but it was not so; in reality it was sold for five hundred dirhams. Hearing the first news, the shafi' surrendered due to deeming the price great, then his shuf'ah is not void (he retains it). And if he was informed that the house was sold for a thousand dirhams, whereas it was sold for wheat or barley or any measurable, weighable, or similar numerable, and the value of that measurable etc. is a thousand dirhams, and the shafi' having heard the first news surrendered, then his surrender is void and his right of shuf'ah remains, because in the second case the reason for surrender could be that providing a thousand dirhams is difficult while giving wheat etc. is easy for him, especially for farmers giving cash is heavy while giving grain is easy. And since similar numerable is on the level of measurable/weighable, it will have the same ruling. Further clarification of the mentioned issue: Contrary to the case when he learns that it was sold for a commodity ('ard) whose value is a thousand or more, because the obligation there is the value (qimah), and that is dirhams or dinars. And if it becomes apparent that it was sold for dinars whose value is a thousand, then there is no shuf'ah for him, and likewise if it is more. And Zufar said he has shuf'ah due to difference of genus. Our proof is that the genus is united (the same) in terms of being thamaniyyah (price/currency). Translation: Contrary to the case when the shafi' learns that the house was sold for a commodity whose value is a thousand or more, because the obligation there is the value (qimah), and that is dirhams and dinars. And if it becomes apparent that the house was sold for dinars whose value is a thousand, then there is no shuf'ah for him, and likewise if it is more. And Zufar said he has shuf'ah due to difference of genus. Our proof is that the genus is the same in terms of being thamaniyyah. Explanation: The shafi' first learned that the house was sold for a thousand dirhams and he surrendered. Then he learned that it was sold for a commodity other than measurable/weighable, whose value is a thousand dirhams or more. Then his surrender is valid and he will not have shuf'ah, because a thousand dirhams and that commodity with a thousand dirhams value are one and the same in terms of property, because in the case of commodity the value is what is due, and that value is what he already knew, i.e., a thousand dirhams. And if the shafi' first learned that the house was sold for a thousand dirhams and surrendered, then later learned that it was sold for dinars whose value is a thousand dirhams or more, then according to the Three Imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad), there is no shuf'ah for him, because the genus is the same in terms of thamaniyyah; for this reason in zakat one is added to the other. And Imam Zufar says he has shuf'ah due to difference of genus, because disparity and excess/shortfall between them is permissible, which indicates difference of genus. The ruling of different genus has already been given that shuf'ah does not fall. This issue is expanded in Majma' al-Anhur vol. 2 p. 466.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof from the Hanafi school concerning situations where a shafi' surrenders his right based on incorrect information about the price or the kind of price (currency/commodity). The ruling is taken from Imam al-Quduri's matn, explained in the sharh. The passage covers several subcases. First, if the shafi' is told the price is a thousand dirhams and surrenders, but later finds the actual price is less (e.g., 500 dirhams), his surrender is void because his decision was based on a false assumption of a higher price. Second, if the price is actually in a different genus (e.g., wheat or barley) of equal or greater value, his surrender is also void, because the genus of the price (mithli) is different from what he was told (dirhams). This applies to measurable (makil), weighable (mawzun), and similar numerable (adadi mutaqarib) items, because their value may be the same but the difficulty of providing one over the other affects his incentive. Third, and crucially, if the actual price is a commodity ('ard) whose value equals the dirhams he was told about, then his surrender is valid and he loses the right, because the obligation in such a sale is the value (qimah), which is dirhams or dinars. Similarly, if the actual price is in gold dinars, according to the Hanafi school (the Three Imams) his surrender is valid because dirhams and dinars are of the same genus in terms of being thamaniyyah (price/currency), so there is no mistake about the amount of monetary value. Imam Zufar dissents and says the shafi' retains the right because dirhams and dinars are different genera, as evidenced by the permissibility of paying one for the other in sarf (exchange) with disparity. The Hanafi proof is that in respect of thamaniyyah, both are the same genus; this is why in zakat, one can be included with the other to complete the nisab. The mufta bihi position is the majority view of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad (and the Hanafi school) that surrender is valid and shuf'ah is lost when the actual price is in a different form of currency (dirhams vs. dinars) or in a commodity equivalent in value. The passage stresses that the key is whether the reported price and the actual price are of the same genus in terms of being a medium of exchange (thamaniyyah) or not.

Key Terms
مكيل (makil): Measurable by volume (e.g., wheat, barley, dates).
موزون (mawzun): Weighable (e.g., gold, silver, oil).
عددي متقارب (adadi mutaqarib): Numerable items that are similar in value and individual units (e.g., eggs, nuts).
الثمنية (al-thamaniyyah): The quality of being a price or medium of exchange (dirhams and dinars).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A shafi' is told that a property was sold for 1000 dirhams and he surrenders. He later discovers it was actually sold for a camel (a non-mithli 'ard) worth 1000 dirhams. According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling?
A) Surrender is void; shuf'ah remains.
B) Surrender is valid; shuf'ah lost.
C) Surrender is void only if camel is mawzun.
D) Surrender is valid only if the camel is of the same breed as the shafi's.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, surrender of shuf'ah is void if the actual price differs in amount or in the genus of mithli (makil/mawzun/adadi mutaqarib); it is valid if the actual price is a non-mithli commodity of equal value or gold dinars of equal value, due to the concept of thamaniyyah.

Stratagem to Drop Pre-emption by Retaining a Strip
Ruling on a legal stratagem to drop shuf'ah by retaining a strip of land.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on using a stratagem (hilah) to sever adjacency and thereby remove the right of pre-emption (shuf'ah), and apply it to similar scenarios.

Core Idea
Pre-emption requires a shared boundary; sever it by keeping a strip, and the neighbor loses his right.

This passage from al-Quduri presents a practical legal device to avoid a harmful pre-emptor. By retaining a small strip of land (one cubit) along the border, the seller severs the adjacency that gives rise to shuf'ah. The Hanafi school permits this stratagem as a legitimate means to prevent harm, based on the principle that a partner takes precedence over a neighbor.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The seller sells all but one cubit along the pre-emptor's boundary. The ruling: no shuf'ah.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is the severance of adjacency (inqita' al-jawar) and the rule that a partner (sharik) outranks a neighbor (jar).
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Shuf'ah requires a common boundary; if the sold portion does not touch the pre-emptor's land, the condition is absent.
4
Note the alternative method
Gifting the strip also works because gifts are not subject to shuf'ah and the donee becomes a partner.
5
Evaluate the mufta bihi
The Hanafi school accepts this hilah; it is mubah (permissible) and relied upon for fatwa.

Source Text

قال و اذا باع دارا الا مقدار ذراع منها فى طول الحد الذى يلى الشفيع فلا شفعة له لانقطاع الجوار وهذه حيلة وكذا اذا وهب منه هذا المقدار و سلّمه اليه لما بينا
ترجمہ امام قدوری نے کہا اور جبکہ بیچ دیا گھر کو مگر اس میں سے ایک ذراع کی مقدار کو اس پٹی کے طول میں جو شفیع سے ملی ہوئی ہے تو اس کیلئے شفعہ نہ ہوگا جوار کے منقطع ہو جانے کی وجہ سے اور یہ ایک حیلہ ہے ( اسقاط شفعہ کا ) اور ایسے ہی جبکہ اس مقدار کو مشتری ہبہ کر چکا ہو اور اس مقدار کو مشتری کے سپرد کر دیا ہو اس دلیل کی وجہ سے جس کو ہم بیان کر چکے ہیں۔
تشریح بسا اوقات شفیع اتنا بد معاش اور فاسق ہوتا ہے کہ جس کا لوگوں کو تکلیف پہنچانا امر معروف ہے تو اگر یہاں اس سے خلاصی کی کوئی صورت نہ ہوگی تو اسے ضر کثیر پہنچنے کا یقین ہے تو اس فصل کے اندر کچھا ایسے حیلے اور تدابیر مذکور ہیں جن سے شفیع کا حق شفعہ باطل ہو جائے گا۔ مثلاً بائع اور مشتری جس گھر کو بچنا چاہتے ہیں اس کے جوار میں بہت شریر آدمی آباد ہے اگر اس نے شفعہ کا دعوی کر دیا تو تمام پڑوسیوں کو تکلیف پہنچائے گا تو بائع کو چاہئے کہ اپنا سارا گھر مشتری کو فروخت کر دئے۔ مگر جس جانب سے وہ مکان شفیع کے مکان سے ملا ہوا ہے ادھر سے پوری پٹی ایک ہاتھ کے بقدر چھوڑ دے یعنی اس کو فروخت نہ کرے تو اب وہ جار گھر کے اندر شفعہ کا مستحق نہ ہوگا۔ کیونکہ جتنا حصہ فروخت ہوا ہے اس حصہ کا اس کی ملکیت سے اتصال نہیں ہے۔ اور جب بائع باقی کو مشتری کے ہاتھ فروخت کرے گا تب بھی شفیع کو شفعہ نہیں ملے گا۔ کیونکہ شفیع محض جار ہے اور مشتری شریک ہے اور ماقبل میں گذر چکا ہے کہ شریک جار پر مقدم ہوتا ہے اسی طرح مذکورہ مقدار مالک و مشتری کو ہبہ کر کے مشتری کے قبضہ میں دے دے تو ہبہ کے اندر شفعہ نہیں ملتا اور اتصال بھی نہیں ہے پھر باقی کو مشتری کے ہاتھ فروخت کر دے تو شفیع کو شفعہ نہیں ملے گا کیونکہ شریک جار پر مقدم ہے۔
He said: And when he sells a house except a measure of one cubit from it in the length of the border that is adjacent to the pre-emptor, then there is no right of pre-emption for him due to the severance of adjacency, and this is a stratagem. Similarly when he gifts this measure to him and delivers it to him, for the reason we have stated. Translation: Imam al-Quduri said: And when a house is sold except a measure of one cubit from it in the length of the strip that adjoins the pre-emptor, then there is no pre-emption for him due to the severance of adjacency, and this is a stratagem (to drop pre-emption). And similarly when that measure has been gifted by the buyer and that measure has been handed over to the buyer, for the reason we have explained. Commentary: Sometimes the pre-emptor is so ill-mannered and immoral that causing trouble to people is his habit. If there is no way to get rid of him, then surely great harm will occur. So in this section, some such stratagems and devices are mentioned by which the pre-emptor's right of pre-emption becomes void. For example, if in the vicinity of the house that the seller and buyer want to sell, a very wicked person resides, if he claims pre-emption, he will cause trouble to all neighbors. So the seller should sell his entire house to the buyer, but from the side where that house adjoins the pre-emptor's house, he should leave a full strip of the measure of one cubit (i.e., not sell it). Then that neighbor will not be entitled to pre-emption in the house, because the portion that was sold does not connect to his property. And when the seller later sells the remaining portion to the buyer, even then the pre-emptor will not get pre-emption, because the pre-emptor is merely a neighbor, and the buyer is a partner, and it has passed earlier that a partner takes precedence over a neighbor. Similarly, if the above-mentioned measure is gifted by the owner to the buyer and delivered to the buyer's possession, then pre-emption does not apply to a gift, and there is no connection; then when the rest is sold to the buyer, the pre-emptor will not get pre-emption because the partner is preferred over the neighbor.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from Imam al-Quduri on a legal stratagem (hilah) to drop the right of pre-emption (shuf'ah). The seller sells the entire house except a small strip (one cubit) along the edge adjoining the pre-emptor's property. Since the sold portion no longer shares a boundary with the pre-emptor, the adjacency (jawar) is severed, and the right of shuf'ah does not arise. The commentary explains that this is necessary when the pre-emptor is harmful; the stratagem protects the buyer from harassment. The same effect is achieved if the seller gifts that strip to the buyer before selling the rest: gifts are not subject to pre-emption, and when the rest is sold later, the buyer is a partner (sharik) in the gifted portion, and a partner takes precedence over a mere neighbor in pre-emption. Thus the pre-emptor's right is defeated.

Key Terms
شفعة (shuf'ah): The right of pre-emption allowing a co-owner or neighbour to purchase a sold property by paying the price.
حيلة (hīlah): A legal stratagem or device used to achieve a permissible outcome while avoiding a harmful or difficult ruling.
جار (jār): A neighbour whose property adjoins the sold property; one of the categories entitled to shuf'ah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the right of pre-emption (shuf'ah) not apply when the seller retains a strip of one cubit adjoining the pre-emptor's property?
A) Because the pre-emptor is no longer a neighbor after the sale.
B) Because adjacency is severed, and pre-emption requires direct boundary contact.
C) Because the pre-emptor consents to the sale.
D) Because the strip is gifted, not sold.

Key Takeaway
Pre-emption requires a common boundary; by retaining a strip, the seller severs adjacency, and the right of shuf'ah falls away — a permissible Hanafi stratagem.

The Stratagem of Price Disparity in Pre-emption
Ruling on a stratagem where the buyer buys a small share for a large price to deter pre-emption

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the stratagem of buying a share at a high price to deter pre-emption and explain the legal basis for its permissibility.

Core Idea
Buying a tiny share at an exorbitant price and the rest cheaply exploits the partner's priority to deter the neighbor's pre-emption.

In Hanafi fiqh, pre-emption (shuf'a) gives a neighbor the right to buy a sold property at the price paid by the buyer. However, if the buyer first acquires a share and becomes a partner, the partner's right of pre-emption outranks the neighbor's. This lesson examines a permissible stratagem that uses this hierarchy to avoid a harmful pre-emptor.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conflict
The pre-emptor (neighbor) has a right over both shares if bought separately, but the buyer becomes a partner in the second purchase.
Partner's right takes precedence over neighbor's.
2
State the principle
A partner's right of pre-emption is stronger than a neighbor's. Therefore, the neighbor cannot claim pre-emption on a share bought by a partner.
This is a well-established Hanafi rule.
3
Describe the stratagem
The buyer purchases the first share for almost the entire property price (e.g., all but one dirham) and the remaining share for a nominal amount.
This makes the first share prohibitively expensive for the neighbor to claim.
4
Explain the result
The neighbor will not claim pre-emption on the first share due to the exorbitant price, and he has no right on the second share (buyer is partner). Thus pre-emption is effectively dropped.
The buyer acquires the whole property without interference.
5
Note the legal status
The stratagem is mubah (permissible) because it uses the established rules of pre-emption and price formation; it does not involve fraud or deception.
Other schools may view this as circumvention, but Hanafis permit it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال واذا ابتاع منها سهما بثمن ثم ابتاع بقيتها فالشفعة للجار فى السهم الاول دون الثاني لان الشفيع جار فيهما الا ان المشترى فى الثانى شريك فيتقدم عليه فان أراد الحيلة ابتاع السهم بالثمن الادرهما مثلا والباقي بالباقي
ترجمہ امام قدوری نے فرمایا اور جبکہ خریدا گھر کا ایک حصہ ثمن کے بدلہ میں پھر خرید لیا گھر کے باقی حصہ کو تو پڑوسی کیلئے پہلے حصہ کے اندر شفعہ ہے دوسرے میں نہیں اس لئے کہ شفیع تو ان دونوں میں جار ہے مگر مشتری دوسرے میں شریک ہے تو مشتری جار پر مقدم ہوگا پس اگر ارادہ کرے حیلہ کا تو ایک حصہ کو پورے ثمن کے بدلے خریدے علاوہ ایک درہم کے اور باقی کو باقی کے بدلہ میں ۔ تشریح مشتری نے گھر کا ایک حصہ خرید لیا پھر دوسری مرتبہ میں باقی گھر کو خرید لیا تو اب شفیع فقط پہلے حصہ میں شفعہ کا دعوی کر سکتا ہے کیونکہ شفیع تو ان دونوں کا پڑوسی ہے مگر مشتری دوسرے حصہ میں شریک ہے اور شریک جار پر مقدم ہوا کرتا ہے۔ پھر اسقاط شفعہ کا بہترین حیلہ بیان فرمایا مثلا گھر کی قیمت میں ہزار روپے تو مشتری گھر کے ایک حصہ کو ۱۹ ہزار نو سو نناوے روپے میں خرید لے اور باقی کو ایک روپے میں تو باقی کو خریدنے کی صورت میں تو شفیع کو شفعہ ملے گا نہیں کیونکہ مشتری جو شریک ہے وہ اس سے مقدم ہے اور پہلے حصہ میں شفیع شفعہ کو طلب کرنے کی جرات نہیں کرے گا۔ ورنہ مثلاً پانچ سوروپے کی مالیت کے انہیں سوننانوے روپے دینے پڑیں گے تو شفیع کے شر سے چھٹکارامل جائے گا۔
He said: And when he buys a share from it with a price, then buys the rest of it, then the pre-emption is for the neighbor in the first share, not the second, because the pre-emptor is a neighbor in both, but the buyer in the second is a partner, so he takes precedence over him. So if he wants a stratagem, he buys the share for the price except one dirham, for example, and the rest for the remainder. Translation: Imam al-Quduri said: And when a share of the house is bought with a price, then the remaining part of the house is bought, then the neighbor has pre-emption in the first share, not the second, because the pre-emptor is a neighbor in both, but the buyer in the second is a partner, so the buyer (as partner) takes precedence over the neighbor. So if he intends a stratagem, he buys one share for the full price except one dirham, and the rest for the remainder. Commentary: The buyer bought a share of the house, then in a second transaction bought the rest. Now the pre-emptor can only claim pre-emption in the first share, because the pre-emptor is a neighbor in both, but the buyer in the second share is a partner, and a partner takes precedence over a neighbor. Then the best stratagem to drop pre-emption is described: for example, the house price is 1000 rupees, so the buyer buys one share for 19,999 rupees and the rest for 1 rupee. Then for the rest purchase, the pre-emptor will not get pre-emption because the buyer (as partner) takes precedence, and for the first share, the pre-emptor will not dare to claim pre-emption because he would have to pay the exorbitant price (19,999 rupees for a small share). This way he is saved from the harm of the pre-emptor.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling on a stratagem for dropping pre-emption when the buyer wishes to acquire the entire property gradually. If the buyer first buys a share and then later buys the remaining share, the pre-emptor (neighbor) has the right of pre-emption only over the first share, because in the second purchase the buyer becomes a partner (sharik) in the property, and a partner's right of pre-emption takes precedence over a neighbor's right. To completely eliminate the pre-emptor's claim, a stratagem is recommended: the buyer purchases the first share for almost the entire price of the property (e.g., all but one dirham) and the remaining share for a nominal amount. Now the pre-emptor, if he wishes to exercise pre-emption over the first share, must pay the exorbitant price, which he will likely avoid, and he has no right in the second share because the buyer is a partner. Thus the pre-emptor's right is effectively dropped.

Key Terms
شفعة (shuf'a): Pre-emption; the right of a neighbor or partner to buy the sold property at the price paid by the buyer.
سهم (sahm): A share or portion of the property; one of the two parts bought separately.
ثمن (thaman): Price or consideration paid for the property.
شريك (sharīk): A partner in ownership of the property; such a person has a stronger right of pre-emption than a neighbor.
حيلة (hīla): A stratagem or lawful legal trick to achieve a permissible end while following the letter of the law.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the stratagem described, why does the buyer buy the first share for almost the entire price?
A) To make the pre-emptor pay a high price if he claims pre-emption.
B) To ensure the seller gets full payment quickly.
C) To avoid the need for a second sale.
D) To make the pre-emptor a partner.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits a stratagem where a buyer purchases a share at an exorbitant price and the remainder cheaply to effectively drop a neighbor's pre-emption right.

Stratagem of Selling with Cloth Instead of Cash
Ruling on a stratagem where the property is sold at an inflated price paid in cloth

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi stratagem of selling property at an inflated price with a separate cloth contract to defeat pre-emption, and identify why it is permissible.

Core Idea
A separate contract for cloth does not affect the price for pre-emption — the pre-emptor must pay the stated price.

This lesson examines a clever Hanafi stratagem that allows a seller to block a troublesome pre-emptor by structuring the sale as two separate contracts. The key legal principle is that pre-emption attaches only to the original sale price, not to subsequent independent transactions. Understanding this stratagem reveals how Hanafi fiqh uses legal formalities to achieve legitimate ends.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problem
A seller wants to sell to a specific buyer but a neighbor or partner has pre-emption right (shuf'ah). The seller needs a way to make pre-emption impractical.
The problem is that the pre-emptor can take the property by paying the sale price.
2
Understand the stratagem structure
The seller sells the property at a price much higher than its actual value. Then, in a separate contract, the buyer sells cloth to the seller worth the actual value. The net effect is that the seller receives the actual value, but the recorded sale price is inflated.
The cloth transaction is a separate contract ('aqd akhar), not part of the original sale.
3
Identify the legal principle
Pre-emption is based on the price stated in the original sale contract. Since the cloth transaction is separate, it does not affect the price for shuf'ah. The pre-emptor must pay the inflated price to take the property.
The text states: 'الشفعة بالثمن دون الثوب لانه عقد آخر'.
4
Note the scope
This stratagem works for both a neighbor and a partner (sharik), unlike the first stratagem (selling with a reserved strip) which only works for a neighbor. The principle applies regardless of relationship.
The author explicitly says: 'تعم الجوار والشركة'.
5
Recognize permissibility
The stratagem is permissible (mubah) under Hanafi fiqh. It is not considered fraud because the contracts are legitimate and separate. The purpose is to avoid harm from a vexatious pre-emptor.
Other schools may disagree, but the Hanafi position is well-established in Zahir al-Riwayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وان ابتاعها بثمن ثم دفع اليه ثوبا عوضاعته فالشفعة بالثمن دون الثوب لانه عقد آخر والثمن هو العوض عن الدار قال رضى الله عنه وهذه حيلة اخرى تعم الجوار والشركة فيباع باضعاف قيمته ويعطى بها ثوب بقدر قيمته
ترجمہ اور اگر گھر کو خرید امن کے بدلہ پھر بائع کوشن کے بدلہ کپڑا دے دیا تو شفعہ ثمن کے بدلہ ہوگا نہ کہ کپڑے کے اسلئے کہ یہ ( کپڑا دینا ) دوسرا عقد ہے اور ثمن ہی گھر کا عوض ہے۔ حضرت مصنف رضی اللہ عنہ نے فرمایا اور یہ دوسرا حیلہ ہے جو جوار اور شرکت دونوں کو عام ہے۔ پس گھر کو بیچ دیا جائے اس کی قیمت کے کئی گنے کے بدلہ اور مبیع کی قیمت کے بقدر کپڑا دے دیا جائے۔ تشریح مثلا گھر کی قیمت تین ہزار درہم ہیں اور گھر کو میں ہزار درہم میں فروخت کیا گیا اور پھر بیس ہزار کے بدلہ کچھ کپڑے لے لیئے گئے جن کی قیمت تین ہزار ہے تو اس سے شفعہ ساقط ہو سکتا ہے کیونکہ اگر شفیع شفعہ میں گھر کو لینا چاہتا ہے تو ۲۰ ہزار درا ہم دے کر لے سکتا ہے کپڑے کے بدلے میں نہیں۔ اسلئے کہ کپڑوں کے لین دین کا مسئلہ یہ بائع اور مشتری کے درمیان الگ عقد ہے اصل عقد سے اس کا کوئی تعلق نہیں تو گھر کا شمن ۲۰ ہزار دراہم ہیں نہ کہ تین ہزار یا کپڑے تو اتنا مہنگا خریدنے کی جرات نہ کرے گا اور یہ حیلہ جار اور شریک دونوں میں چلے گا اور پہلا حیلہ فقط جار کیلئے ہوگا۔
And if he buys it with a price, then gives him a cloth as its substitute, then pre-emption is based on the price, not the cloth, because this (giving cloth) is another contract, and the price is the consideration for the house. The author (may Allah be pleased with him) said: This is another stratagem that covers both neighbor and partnership. So the house is sold for multiples of its value, and a cloth of its value is given. Translation: And if the house is bought with a price, then the seller gives cloth as compensation for it, then pre-emption is based on the price, not the cloth, because this (giving cloth) is another contract, and the price is the consideration for the house. The author (may Allah be pleased with him) said: This is another stratagem that covers both neighbor and partnership. So the house is sold for several times its value, and a cloth equivalent to its value is given. Commentary: For example, the house price is 3000 dirhams, and the house is sold for 20,000 dirhams, and then some cloth worth 3000 dirhams is taken in exchange. Then pre-emption can be dropped because if the pre-emptor wants to take the house via pre-emption, he can take it by paying 20,000 dirhams, not the cloth. Because the transaction of cloth is a separate contract between buyer and seller, unrelated to the original contract. So the price of the house is 20,000 dirhams, not 3000 or the cloth. Then he will not dare to buy it so expensively. This stratagem works for both neighbor and partner, while the first stratagem was only for the neighbor.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage presents a second stratagem that works for both a neighbor and a partner. The seller sells the property at a price significantly higher than its actual value (e.g., 20,000 dirhams) but then, in a separate contract, buys cloth from the buyer worth the actual value (3,000 dirhams). The pre-emptor, if he wishes to exercise shuf'ah, must pay the stated price of 20,000 dirhams — he cannot substitute the cloth because that is a separate agreement. Since the price is exorbitant, the pre-emptor will likely not claim his right. Unlike the first stratagem (selling with a reserved strip), which only works for a neighbor, this stratagem works for both a neighbor and a partner, because the principle applies regardless of the relationship: the partner's right is also based on the sale price. The stratagem is permissible because it uses the formality of separate contracts to achieve a legitimate goal of avoiding a harmful pre-emptor.

Key Terms
ثوب (thawb): Cloth used as a substitute payment in a separate contract; it is not part of the original sale price.
عوض (iwaḍ): Compensation or substitute; here, the cloth is given as compensation for the price.
شركة (shirkah): Partnership; this stratagem works for a partner (not just a neighbor).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the pre-emptor have to pay the inflated price (e.g., 20,000 dirhams) and not the cloth value?
A) Because the cloth transaction is a separate contract, not part of the original sale.
B) Because the cloth is not valuable enough.
C) Because the pre-emptor is a neighbor, not a partner.
D) Because the seller prefers cash.

Key Takeaway
The stratagem of selling at an inflated price with a separate cloth contract is a permissible Hanafi device to defeat pre-emption because pre-emption is based on the sale price alone.

Ruling on Shuf'ah for Partial Purchases and a Stratagem to Defeat the Neighbor's Pre-Emption
States ruling on shuf'ah for partial purchases and a hilah to defeat it

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on shuf'ah when a property is purchased in parts and identify the hilah to defeat the neighbor's right of pre-emption.

Core Idea
A partner's right in shuf'ah takes precedence over a neighbor's, enabling a stratagem that deters the neighbor's claim.

Pre-emption (shuf'ah) allows a neighbor or partner to step into a sale. But what happens when the buyer purchases the property in pieces? This lesson clarifies that the neighbor's right is limited when the buyer becomes a partner, and it reveals a clever legal stratagem to completely avoid the neighbor's claim.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the parties
Recognize the scenario: A buyer (mushtari) purchases a house in two separate transactions from the same seller. The neighbor (jaar) is a potential pre-emptor (shafi').
2
Apply the precedence rule
In the first transaction, the neighbor has shuf'ah because he is a neighbor. In the second transaction, the buyer becomes a partner (sharik) in the house, and a partner's right to pre-emption is stronger than a neighbor's. Thus, the neighbor loses shuf'ah on the second part.
This ruling is based on the Hanafi principle that a partner takes precedence over a neighbor.
3
Understand the hilah (stratagem)
To completely defeat the neighbor's shuf'ah, the buyer purchases the first part at an exorbitant price (almost the full value) and the second part for a nominal sum. The neighbor will not dare to claim shuf'ah on the first part because he would have to pay the high price, and on the second part he has no right due to the buyer's partnership.
The hilah exploits the neighbor's lack of incentive to claim an overpriced portion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الشفعة
ترجمہ امام قدوری نے فرمایا اور جبکہ خریدا گھر کا ایک حصہ ثمن کے بدلہ میں پھر خرید لیا گھر کے باقی حصہ کو تو پڑوسی کیلئے پہلے حصہ کے اندر شفعہ ہے دوسرے میں نہیں اس لئے کہ شفیع تو ان دونوں میں جار ہے مگر مشتری دوسرے میں شریک ہے تو مشتری جار پر مقدم ہوگا پس اگر ارادہ کرے حیلہ کا تو ایک حصہ کو پورے ثمن کے بدلے خریدے علاوہ ایک درہم کے اور باقی کو باقی کے بدلہ میں ۔ تشریح مشتری نے گھر کا ایک حصہ خرید لیا پھر دوسری مرتبہ میں باقی گھر کو خرید لیا تو اب شفیع فقط پہلے حصہ میں شفعہ کا دعوی کر سکتا ہے کیونکہ شفیع تو ان دونوں کا پڑوسی ہے مگر مشتری دوسرے حصہ میں شریک ہے اور شریک جار پر مقدم ہوا کرتا ہے۔ پھر اسقاط شفعہ کا بہترین حیلہ بیان فرمایا مثلا گھر کی قیمت میں ہزار روپے تو مشتری گھر کے ایک حصہ کو ۱۹ ہزار نو سو نناوے روپے میں خرید لے اور باقی کو ایک روپے میں تو باقی کو خریدنے کی صورت میں تو شفیع کو شفعہ ملے گا نہیں کیونکہ مشتری جو شریک ہے وہ اس سے مقدم ہے اور پہلے حصہ میں شفیع شفعہ کو طلب کرنے کی جرات نہیں کرے گا۔ ورنہ مثلاً پانچ سوروپے کی مالیت کے انہیں سوننانوے روپے دینے پڑیں گے تو شفیع کے شر سے چھٹکارامل جائے گا۔
Book of Pre-emption. Translation: Imam Quduri said: When a part of a house is purchased for a price, then the remaining part of the house is purchased, the neighbor has pre-emption in the first part, not in the second, because the pre-emptor is a neighbor to both, but the buyer is a partner in the second part, so the buyer takes precedence over the neighbor. So if he intends a stratagem, he should purchase one part for the full price except one dirham, and the remaining for the remainder. Explanation: The buyer purchased one part of the house, then in a second instance purchased the remaining house. Now the pre-emptor can only claim pre-emption in the first part, because the pre-emptor is a neighbor to both, but the buyer is a partner in the second part, and a partner takes precedence over a neighbor. Then the best stratagem for dropping pre-emption is stated: for example, the house's value is one thousand rupees; the buyer buys one part of the house for 19,999 rupees and the remaining for one rupee. In the case of buying the remainder, the pre-emptor will not get pre-emption because the buyer who is a partner has precedence over him, and the pre-emptor will not dare to demand pre-emption in the first part, otherwise, for example, he would have to pay 99 rupees for something worth 500 rupees, so he will be rid of the evil of the pre-emptor.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage from Kitab al-Shuf'ah explains the ruling when a buyer purchases a house in separate transactions. The text first states the ruling: if a buyer buys part of a house, then later buys the remaining part, the neighbor (shafi') only has pre-emption (shuf'ah) on the first part, not the second. The reason is that the shafi' is a neighbor to both parts, but the buyer (mushtari) becomes a partner (sharik) in the second part. In Hanafi law, a partner has a stronger right to pre-emption than a mere neighbor. The text then provides a hilah (legal stratagem) to completely defeat the shafi's right: the buyer purchases the first part at an exorbitant price (just shy of the full value) and the second part for a nominal amount. The shafi' will not claim shuf'ah on the first part because its price is too high, and on the second part he has no right due to the buyer's partnership. This stratagem is specifically for a neighbor (jaar), not a partner. The commentary emphasizes that the partner's precedence is an established principle in the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
شفعة (shuf'ah): The right of pre-emption: the right of a co-owner or neighbor to purchase a sold property by substituting the buyer and paying the price.
جار (jaar): A neighbor who has a right of pre-emption in Hanafi law due to adjacency.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
مشتری نے گھر کا ایک حصہ خریدا، پھر باقی حصہ خرید لیا۔ شفیع کس حصے میں شفعہ کا دعوی کر سکتا ہے؟
A) صرف دوسرے حصے میں
B) صرف پہلے حصے میں
C) دونوں حصوں میں
D) کسی میں نہیں

Key Takeaway
When a property is purchased in parts, the neighbor's shuf'ah is limited to the first part; the buyer's partnership in the second part overrides the neighbor, and a hilah can completely defeat the neighbor's claim.

Hilah Using Cloth Substitute to Avoid Shuf'ah
Explains a legal stratagem using an inflated price and separate cloth transaction to deter shuf'ah.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the mechanism of the cloth hilah for avoiding shuf'ah and state why the pre-emptor must pay the inflated price.

Core Idea
The pre-emptor must pay the inflated price, not the cloth, because the cloth transaction is a separate contract.

We explore a creative legal stratagem (hilah) used to deter a pre-emptor from exercising shuf'ah. The stratagem involves selling the property at an inflated price and then giving cloth equivalent to the actual value in a separate transaction. The key legal point is that shuf'ah attaches to the price (thaman) of the original sale, not to the cloth, because the cloth is part of a distinct contract. This lesson dissects the mechanism, its effectiveness, and the hidden risk if the property is later reclaimed by a rightful owner.

Guided Path
1
State the objective of the hilah
Identify the goal: to make shuf'ah unattractive to the pre-emptor by inflating the price.
2
Understand the sale structure
The house is sold for an inflated price (e.g., 20,000 dirhams). Then, a separate contract is made where the buyer gives cloth worth the actual value (e.g., 3,000 dirhams) to the seller.
The cloth price is not part of the original sale price.
3
Apply the separate contract rule
Shuf'ah only applies to the price of the original sale (thaman). The cloth transaction is a separate 'aqd and does not affect the thaman.
Thus, if the pre-emptor exercises shuf'ah, he must pay the full inflated price (20,000 dirhams), not the cloth's value.
4
Recognize the risk of property reclamation
If a third party proves rightful ownership (mustahaqq), the buyer recovers the inflated price from the seller, causing the seller loss.
The seller received only cloth (worth 3,000) but must return 20,000 dirhams.
